==PAGE:1==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Master Thesis
Interpretation of Monitoring Data of Deep
Excavations from Metro Cityringen
Author:
Adriana C. Hern´andez Guardado, s161094
Supervisors:
Varvara Zania, Associate Professor at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU)
Francesco Petrella, Associate Director at ARUP
June 23, 2018
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
There is an increasing demand for underground space in largely urbanized areas. In this environ-
ment, deep excavations often have to be constructed in proximity to nearby sensitive structures
and may induce ground movement that could compromise the integrity of these. Hence, the
prediction of lateral wall deﬂections and ground movements is important in the design crite-
rion to avoid damages to adjacent buildings and other structures. Through the analysis and
interpretation of the monitoring data of seven deep excavations for the upcoming metro line
in Copenhagen, Cityringen, this thesis shows that the current empirical relationships fail to
describe mixed and stiﬀsoils.
The design wall deformations proved to be highly conservative, mainly due to the use of
lower bound design geotechnical parameters. This research presents an empirical correlation to
predict the maximum lateral wall deﬂection taking into account the following factors:
• The bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall, EI;
• The soil stiﬀness, Es;
• The weighted average vertical spacing between horizontal supports, havg.
This thesis uses the data of the seven stations to propose a new relative system stiﬀness
parameter which helps to describe the response of the retaining wall in this particular stratigra-
phy. Lastly, a pseudo-ﬁnite element simulation is carried out to back-analyze the soil stiﬀness,
focusing on the Copenhagen Limestone layers.
i
==PAGE:3==
Preface
This Master thesis was carried out from January the 2nd to June the 23rd at the Department of
Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark under supervision of Associate Pro-
fessor Varvara Zania and Francesco Petrella, Associate Director at ARUP. The thesis accounts
for 30 ECTS points and is written in fulﬁllment to the requirements for obtaining a MSc. degree
in Civil Engineering.
English title
Interpretation of monitoring data of deep excavations from metro Cityringen
Danish title
Opfattelse af overv˚agnings data af dyb udgravninger fra metro Cityringen

Acknowledgements
I would like to express my deepest gratitude to my advisor, Associate Professor Varvara Zania,
for her ongoing support not only during my Master thesis, but all through my studies at the
Technical University of Denmark. It was been one of the greatest learning opportunities and I
am grateful for the guidance and mentorship she has provided me in these two years.
This thesis was carried out in part in collaboration with ARUP Denmark and I would like to
acknowledge and thank Associate Director, Francesco Petrella and Geteochnical Engineer, Mette
Jensen for their technical insight and challenging discussions. The monitoring data and design
reports were provided by CMT engineers at Metroselskabet - to Ivi Arce Juliao and Katrine
Falbe-Hansen, thank you for your support in this challenging task. I extend my gratitude to
the Consejo Nacional de Ciencia y Tecnolog´ıa (CONACyT), for providing the ﬁnancial support
towards my studies in Denmark.
Lastly, this thesis is dedicated to my parents and family, without whom this thesis could not
have been completed; and without whom, I am not complete.

Chapter 1
Introduction
The limited space in urban areas has led to the common practice of underground construction.
In deep excavations, the excessive lateral deformation of the wall is of major concern due to
the impact it may have in nearby structures. Excavation induced ground movements may lead
to displacements and rotations in the foundation systems of adjacent buildings that can cause
signiﬁcant damage.
In Copenhagen, tunneling is common construction practice. The city has one of the most
advanced underground public transport systems in Europe with stations usually located in urban
areas with buildings in the proximity of the excavations.
1.1
Copenhagen Metro System
The Cityringen project is a new circular line in the city center and consists of two 15.5km
tunnels and 17 new stations (two of which are transfer stations) all at 30m below ground level
(Impregilo, 2018). The project is technically complex since the city is at sea level and the stations
will be located in heavily urbanized areas. Two extensions are planned for the Cityringen; the
Nordhavn and Sydhavn extensions will also be part of the Copenhagen metro network. Figure
1.1.1 shows the current and future metro lines in Copenhagen. Københavns Metro is owned by
Metroselskabet.
1
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Figure 1.1.1: Map of the existing and future metro line. Metro lines M1 and M2 are existing and
operating, M3 (Cityringen) to be open in 2019 and future line, M4 (Metro, 2018).
Tunnelling can induce ground movements at and/or below the ground surface. The stations
and tunnels are constructed in close vicinity to existing buildings and structure which at times
date back to the 16th century and are typically built on wooden piles.
The foundations of
these buildings can be aﬀected by minor changes in the in situ ground conditions. In order to
prevent any undesirable and potentially damaging settlements to the nearby structures, building
protection measures, including monitoring, settlement control and the installation of mitigation
measures are of great importance in the Cityringen project (Eskesen et al., 2014).
1.2
Project Description
The Cityrigen connects the areas of Østerbro, Nørrebro and Vesterbro to Frederiksberg and Indre
By (City). This thesis focuses on the data interpretation of 7 stations within the Cityringen
shown in Figure 1.2.1:
• Enghave Plads (EHP)
• Frederiksberg (FB) - Transfer Station
• Aksel Møllers Have (AMH)
• Nuuks Plads (NUP)
• Nørrebros Runddel (NOR)
• Vibenshus Runddel (VHR)
• Østerport (KK)
Page 2
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Figure 1.2.1: Map over the Cityringen (Metro, 2018).
The stations were chosen based on the structural retaining system and their distance to
nearby structures. Typically, the stations are 60x20m with excavations depths up to 25m below
the ground level supported by several levels of struts. These stations utilize secant pile retain-
ing walls embedded enough to minimize groundwater abstraction. The secant piles are 1m in
diameter at approximately 800mm spacing and have an average length of 28m.
The overall geology of the project is described by 1 to 5m thick ﬁll layer, 10 to 30m of Qua-
ternary sand, gravel and clay tills underlain by limestone. Investigations in the entire Cityringen
project reveal that the deposits are highly variable and present a sequence of sand/gravel and
clay till layers (Eskesen et al., 2014). The alignment can be further described as follows:
• The northern part of the site is mainly dominated by meltwater sands and gravels deposited
on the limestone;
• The southwestern area the layers consists mainly of clay till;
• The limestone is uniformly embedded, with ﬂint beds and bioturbated zones. The upper
0 to 4m is locally glacially disturbed and heavily fractured.
The overall geology of the site is shown in Figure 1.2.2.
Page 3
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Figure 1.2.2: Geological cross section of the site (Eskesen et al., 2014).
1.3
Monitoring
Monitoring deep excavations has been common practice in the past decades in order to mit-
igate damages to nearby structures.
Observational data was famously used by Peck (1969)
and it is the bases for the ‘observational method’ through which construction and design pro-
cedures are adjusted based on the ﬁeld observations and measurements made as construction
proceeds (Finno, 2007). Empirical relationships have been proposed in works by Clough, Smith,
et al. (1989) with the use of observational data; this is further discussed in Chapter 2.
Due to its complexity and risks, the Cityringen project is heavily monitored. Baseline moni-
toring was carried out prior to any construction activities while during construction, movement
is monitored through an extensive 3D monitoring system with extonsometers, inclinometers and
piezometers. The system records measurements in real time with a reading frequency of at least
one measurement every two hours.
The retaining walls are monitored by a combination of inclinometers and load monitoring
(strain gauges). Each station has a minimum of eight inclinometers which read daily while the
excavation is proceeding and with enough readings during the permanent works.
Moreover, a database system for integration, storage, analysis and reporting for all monitor-
ing has been developed in order to easily collect and interpret the monitoring data. The database
Kronos is ‘access via a geographical information system (GIS), which gives both Metroselskabet
and the civil works contractor real-time remote access to all data collected’ (Eskesen et al., 2014).
Page 4
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 1. Introduction
The monitoring data is used to identify the performance of the deep excavations and to use
this information to predict the inﬂuence the behaviour of the wall may have in nearby structures
and to further understand the geotechnical properties of the soil.
1.4
Objective and Relevance of Research
As mentioned before, the stratigraphy in the Copenhagen area is relatively homogeneous and
future tunneling projects are planned for the city. Limited data has been reported for the soil-
structure interaction in the city of Copenhagen and the use of the monitoring data, through the
observational method, can aid in the optimization of designs.
With the use of the monitoring data and proper interpretation, this research seeks to es-
tablish a simpliﬁed procedure for the prediction of maximum lateral deﬂection of secant walls.
Furthermore, the study focuses on the back-analysis of soil stiﬀness from the deformed walls
with special care for the Upper Copenhagen Limestone. If the properties of the soil layers and
limestone are better understood, improvements and optimization can be made to supporting
structures having a signiﬁcant economical impact.
The thesis focuses on wall deﬂections resulting solely from the unbalanced forces due to the
removal of soil inside the excavation under plane-strain conditions.
1.5
Content of Thesis
Chapter 2 of this thesis presents a literature review and state-of-the-art summary regarding ex-
cavation induced movements. The chapter discusses the available methods of predicting lateral
wall movements and its limitations.
Chapter 3 presents an overview in the methodology employed to properly extract, sort,
present and interpret/analyze the monitored data provided by the engineers at Metroselskabet.
Chapter 4 discusses the seven stations investigated in this thesis. An overall description of
the station including geological and geotechnical characteristics of the site, water levels, perma-
nent and temporary works and available monitoring data is summarized, in accordance to the
design reports provided by Metroselskabet. The results of the data interpretation are presented
and discussed on the basis of lateral wall movements, excavation depths and system stiﬀness.
Lastly, a brief summary and conclusion is given for each station regarding the system’s behaviour.
Chapter 5 summarizes the ﬁndings of Chapter 4 with the focus of comparing all seven sta-
tions and present a relationship between lateral wall movement and system stiﬀness as well for
a normalized deﬂected shape.
Chapter 6 focuses on the sensitivity analysis of three stations (AMH, KK and VHR) carried
out with the Oasys software, Frew. The chapter discusses the designed parameters used and
the modiﬁed stiﬀness and compares the model results with the actual measurements at given
inclinometers.
Page 5
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2
Literature Review
In congested urban areas, excavations often take place adjacent to existing buildings, pavements,
utilities and other urban facilities. Uncontrolled deformation of such facilities can induce ad-
ditional cost and safety risks to any underground project. Therefore, it is important for the
design of excavation support in this environment to consider deformations that are permissible.
This chapter reviews potential sources of deformation and provides a critical review of existing
literature addressing the problems associated with estimating wall and ground displacements.
2.1
Sources of Deformation
Movements associated with deep excavations are related to a number of factors including (Peck, 1969),
but not limited to:
• Base stability;
• Soil type;
• Elastic compression and rebound of the soils responding to oﬀ-loading;
• Consolidation of loose sands and soft clays arising from pore-water pressure changes;
• Wall type;
• Structural stiﬀness of vertical support elements;
• Horizontal support type;
• Spacing of horizontal supports;
• Construction method;
• Workmanship.
Any of the listed factors may control the overall movement of a supported excavation. Since
the total deformation is a complex soil-structure interaction problem, the quantitative analy-
sis of ground movements associated with excavation supports is diﬃcult (Storer J. Boone, 2003).
Estimation of ground movements associated with supported excavations is generally based
on a combination of analytical and empirical methods combined with judgment and experience.
The potential magnitude of deformation considering some of the above factors are discussed
below as related to the most recent research available.
2.2
Ground Movements and Lateral Deformation
In his state-of-the-art publication, Peck (1969) ﬁrst illustrated the general magnitude and pat-
tern of settlements adjacent to supported excavation. The comprehensive study suggested that
6
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
settlements next to deep excavations correlate to the type of soil as shown in Figure 2.2.1.
Through a series of case histories, mostly sheet pile and soldier pile walls, the author produced
plots of maximum vertical settlement, δvmax, normalized by the excavation depth, Hex, against
distance from the excavation. The data was separated into three zones based on the quality of
workmanship and type of soil:
• Zone I: sand/soft to hard clay/average workmanship;
• Zone II: very soft to soft clay with either a limited depth of soft clay beneath the excavation;
• Zone III: very soft to soft clay with low margin of safety against base heave.
Values for the ratio δvmax/Hex varied between a maximum of 1% to larger than 2% for
Zone III. Peck proposed that the lateral wall movements are associated with settlements of
approximately equal volume (Figure 2.2.2), this concept was later investigated by Clough and
O’Rourke (1990). The author concluded that the absence of recorded settlement suggests the
absence of such for projects in granular soils and that the maximum settlements is usually
< 0.5% of the excavation depth.
Figure 2.2.1: Summary of settlement adjacent to open cuts in various soils as a function of distance
from edge of excavation (Peck, 1969).
Page 7
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Figure 2.2.2: Horizontal and vertical displacements from excavations (Peck, 1969).
Building on the work by Peck (1969), Goldberg et al. (1976) investigated 63 case histories
demonstrating the inﬂuence of not only the soil type, but also the retaining wall type and support
system. The authors concluded that in sands and gravels as well as in stiﬀclays, the maximum
lateral movement in comparison to excavation was δhmax ≤0.35%He. The wall deformation was
also compared in terms of stiﬀness and base stability number. The inverse of the ﬂexibility
number ﬁrst proposed by Rowe et al. (1952):
Sr = EI/H4
(2.2.1)
Where EI is the bending stiﬀness of the wall and H is the average vertical spacing between
supports. Figure 2.2.3 shows the comparison of wall deformations based on empirical data. The
authors included a series of ﬁnite element studies and concluded that ﬁnite element analysis un-
derestimated the lateral wall movements because the model did not consider all the construction
variables that may impact the wall movements.
Figure 2.2.3:
Relationship between wall bending stiﬀness, maximum lateral displacement and soil
strength (taken from Storer J. Boone (2003)).
In Mana et al. (1981), the authors extended the work of Peck (1969), D’Appolonia et
al. (1971) and Goldberg et al. (1976) and presented a procedure for predicting the wall and
soil movement for excavations in clay deposits supported by cross lot braced walls by proposing
Page 8
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
a relationship between maximum lateral movements and potential for basal heave. The study
was based on combining the results of ﬁeld performance and ﬁnite element analysis and on a
linear-elastic, perfectly plastic constitutive model assuming undrained conditions; from this, the
base stability factor of safety based on Terzaghi (1944) was calculated. The authors recognized
that even though the factor of safety obtained in this manner is not rigorously correct of all
conditions, it serves as a convenient index for many cases. Figure 2.2.4 shows the factor of
safety against basal heave as a function of the maximum observed lateral movements for the
case histories normalized by the excavation depth at which they were measured. At high factors
of safety (stiﬀclays or granular soils), the non-dimensional movements are roughly constant at
a value of 0.5%.
Figure 2.2.4: Relationship between factor of safety against basal heave and dimensionless maximum
lateral wall movement for case history after Mana et al. (1981) (taken from Ahmed (2014)).
Mana et al. (1981) proposed a simpliﬁed method for predicting movements of braced cuts
by ﬁnding an initial estimate using Figure 2.2.4 and revise it by using a series of inﬂuence
coeﬃcients:
δ∗
hm = δhmαMαW αSαP αDαB
(2.2.2)
The inﬂuence coeﬃcients, α, account for the following:
• αM: eﬀect of modulus multiplier;
• αW : eﬀect of wall stiﬀness;
• αS: eﬀect of eﬀective strut stiﬀness S to account for beam-column behaviour and connec-
tion compression;
• αP : eﬀect of strut pre-loading;
• αD: eﬀect of depth to underlying ﬁrm layer;
• αB: eﬀect of excavation width on maximum lateral and vertical movements.
Expanding the research made by Mana et al. (1981), Clough, Smith, et al. (1989) presented a
method of estimating the maximum lateral wall movements as a function of the eﬀective system
stiﬀness and the factor of safety against basal heave and is illustrated in Figure 2.2.5.
Page 9
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Figure 2.2.5: Chart for estimating maximum lateral wall movements and ground surface settlements
for support systems in clay as per Clough, Smith, et al. (1989) (taken from Zapata-Medina (2007)).
In a review of state-of-the-art of in situ wall design, Clough and O’Rourke (1990) summarized
the available methods for estimating the maximum ground surface deformation associated with
deep excavations and based on additional case studies they were able to deliver a series of
plots which presented a conservative envelope for ground surface settlements that can occur
around an excavation wall. In this study, eﬀects of the basic excavation and support system are
separated from separate construction activities. The data was evaluated in two categories: still
clays/residual soil/sands and soft to medium clays. Again, for soft clays, the authors investigate
the factor of safety against basal heave as proposed by Clough, Smith, et al. (1989). Furthermore,
the authors presented Figure 2.2.6 to understand movement patters and can be used as design
tools to estimate the maximum wall and soil movements based on the excavation depth, seen in
Figure 2.2.6.
Page 10
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Figure 2.2.6: Observed maximum lateral movements for in situ walls in stiﬀclays, residual soils and
sands (Clough and O’Rourke, 1990).
Clough and O’Rourke (1990) clariﬁed that for the cases where movements fall within the
scatter around the 0.2%H trend line, a roughly linear relationship can be observed and an elastic
behaviour can be assumed for the soil. Following this assumption, the authors performed a series
of ﬁnite element models where the parameters such as the soil and wall stiﬀness were varied,
the results are shown in Figure 2.2.7. The predicted response is consistent with the average
behaviour observed in Figure 2.2.6.
Figure 2.2.7: Predicted maximum lateral wall movements by ﬁnite element analysis modeling for stiﬀ
soil conditions (Clough and O’Rourke, 1990).
The authors also presented the general patterns of ground movements based on inclinometer
and settlement measurements for braced and tied-back excavations, seen in Figure 2.2.8. During
initial stages, the soil may be excavated prior to the installation of a support and this may lead to
a cantilevered-like behaviour. This may also occur is the support does not have enough stiﬀness
or pre-load. As the excavation advances to deeper depth, the upper wall movement is restrained
by the installation of new support and there is a deep inward movement of the wall.
Page 11
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Figure 2.2.8: Typical proﬁles of movement for braced and tied-back walls (Clough and O’Rourke, 1990).
In a case history analysis done by Carder (1995) using the measurements of ground and wall
movements at sites where bored piles and diaphragm walls were used for the construction of
retained cuttings, cut-and-cover tunnels and deep excavations. The author separated the move-
ments caused by wall installation and excavation in front of the wall and the total movement
was then assessed as the summation of both. Carder (1995) found that ground movements in
stiﬀclay due to wall installation varied according to the construction technique. With a top-
down construction and walls installed in stiﬀclay, the maximum movement occurred at depths
of 0.7-0.9Hex having a magnitude of 0.04-0.16% of the excavation depth, Hex.
A relationship between the deformations of excavations in the Taipei area and their excava-
tion depths was investigated by Ou, Hsieh, et al. (1993), the results are shown in Figure 2.2.9.
In most case histories, the deformation of the wall deteriorates with the increase of the excava-
tion depth; the authors emphasize the fact that because most case histories were based on clay,
the upper limit of the proposed equation should be used for clay and the lower limit for sand
(Ou, 2006).
Figure 2.2.9: Relationships between maximum wall deﬂections and excavation depths (Ou, Hsieh, et
al., 1993).
Focusing in investigating the system stiﬀness, Addenbrooke (1994) derived a logical expan-
sion to Rowe et al. (1952) ﬂexibility number by integrating the simple beam equation to ﬁnd
Page 12
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
the displacement over the length and deﬁned a displacement ﬂexibility number, ∆, as:
∆= h5/EI
(2.2.3)
Where h is the average vertical spacing between supports and EI is the bending stiﬀness
of the wall. By keeping ∆constant, Addenbrooke (1994) proposed that the bending moment
reducing beneﬁts from the use of a more ﬂexible wall can be accurately balanced against the con-
struction requirements of more propping levels. TI Addenbrooke et al. (2000) then demonstrated
that this ﬂexibility number deﬁned support systems that will displace to the same maximum lat-
eral wall deﬂection and will result in the same proﬁles of vertical and horizontal ground surface
displacement behind the wall. However, there is little intrinsic theoretical or practical beneﬁts of
the use of this ﬂexibility number rather than a non-dimensional stiﬀness (Storer J. Boone, 2003).
Long (2001) analyzed 256 case histories from Peck (1969) up to date and the study largely
validated the results of Clough and O’Rourke (1990) for stiﬀsoils. The author sought to identify
major trends and lessons to be learned from the data.
Figure 2.2.10 shows the normalized
maximum lateral wall movement, δhmax/Hex, versus excavation depth, Hex showing an evident
scatter in the data. The average δhmax is 0.17%, 0.19% and 0.16% for propped, anchored and
top-down, respectively. The normalized displacement was also plotted against system stiﬀness as
proposed by Clough, Smith, et al. (1989) in Figure 2.2.11 showing a large scattering but a slight
indication of decreasing movement with increasing stiﬀness. Figure 2.2.12 shows the normalized
displacement against the ﬂexibility number as proposed by Addenbrooke (1994), showing wider
scattering.
Figure 2.2.10: Walls with high factor of safety against base heave - normalized maximum lateral move-
ment versus depth (Long, 2001).
Page 13
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Figure 2.2.11:
Walls with high factor of safety against basal heave - normalized maximum lateral
movement versus Clough, Smith, et al. (1989) system stiﬀness (Long, 2001) (taken from Ahmed (2014)).
Figure 2.2.12: Walls with high factor of safety - normalized maximum lateral movement versus Adden-
brooke (1994) ﬂexibility number (Long, 2001).
Long (2001) concluded that the deformations of deep excavations in non-cohesive soils and
stiﬀclays are largely independent of the system stiﬀness. This may be attributed to the fact
that once enough stiﬀness is available in the system, movement is determined by the amount of
base heave and it is also possible that stiﬀsoils have signiﬁcant capacity to ‘arch’ or ‘self-support’.
Moormann (2004) presented an extensive data base of more than 530 worldwide case histo-
ries. Figure 2.2.13 shows the maximum horizontal wall displacement plotted against the exca-
vation depth for ﬁve classes of soil types. The author identiﬁed that for 65% of all case histories
in non-cohesive soils, the measured wall displacements are smaller than δhmax/Hex=0.25%, the
average value was δhmax/Hex=0.27%, similar to that found by Clough and O’Rourke (1990).
Page 14
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Figure 2.2.13: Maximum horizontal wall displacement δhmax versus excavation depth, Hex, depending
on type of soil, retaining wall and support system used (Moormann, 2004).
Tan et al. (2011) monitored a 26m deep top-down excavation in soft clay in downtown Shang-
hai with a comprehensive instrumentation program. The authors found that using the top-down
excavation method with braced diaphragm walls along with jet-grouting reinforcement, the con-
struction of the 26m deep metro station did not induce a signiﬁcant adverse impact on the
urban environment. The wall developed a typical deep-seated inward movement during excava-
tion. The measured maximum wall deﬂections were between δhmax/Hex=0.1-0.5%.
Current design construction approaches are mainly empirical; in granular and layered soils
it may lead to conservative results.
Storer J. Boone (2003) proposed to ﬁll this gap in de-
Page 15
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
sign bases. The author explored fundamental factors related to design of excavations in urban
environments through modelling and detailed investigation of ﬁeld performance. The author
proposed a new method for estimating the total lateral load on ﬂexible retaining structures
which accounts for pressures above and below the excavation depth. The work also included
charts that identiﬁed the transition in earth pressure behaviour from a ‘rigid’ wall design to a
‘ﬂexible’ wall design. One of the more important conclusions was that displacement behaviour
associated with deep excavations is governed greatly by the unload-reload stiﬀness properties of
the soil mass, regardless if the soil is cohesive or non-cohesive. The method of estimated dis-
placement was summarized by S. Boone et al. (2005), the design steps are shown in Table 2.2.1
and design parameters in Figure 2.2.14. The authors found that the results are consistent with
both observation and empirical charts as well for past numerical studies. Comparison between
measured and back-analyzed lateral displacements demonstrated reasonable agreement between
the estimation method and ﬁeld performance (S. Boone et al., 2005).
Figure 2.2.14: Maximum lateral displacements of excavation support systems and modiﬁcation factors
to account for the inﬂuence of support system stiﬀness and factor of safety, construction stage (removal
of supports), depth to a ﬁrm layer, degree of preloading, strut stiﬀness, excavation width, and soil stiﬀness
(S. Boone et al., 2005).
Page 16
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
Table 2.2.1: Summary of displacement estimation equations based on curve-ﬁtting of non-linear numer-
ical modeling results (S. Boone et al., 2005)
Storer J. Boone (2003) also investigated the eﬀect of pre-loading excavations braces. The
author found that this reduced deformation by the following:
• Inducing elastic compression deformations immediately behind the wall system prior to
further excavation;
• Imposing horizontal stresses within the soil mass prior to the stress-redistribution;
• Contributing to the base stability and limiting related deformations below the base of the
excavation.
Building on the work in Clough and O’Rourke (1990), Zapata-Medina (2007) presented a
Page 17
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
new semi-empirical design methodology that facilitates the selection of the excavation support
system, which would limit the excavation-related ground movements. This work was later ex-
panded by Bryson et al. (2011). The authors proposed a revised system stiﬀness factor which
gives more reliable results with the data using the data of 30 case histories that included soft,
medium and stiﬀclay. As part of the proposed methodology, a new parameter was developed,
the relative stiﬀness ratio R, which relates the strength and stiﬀness of the excavation support
system including the three-dimensional eﬀects. The graphical representation of the close-form
relative stiﬀness ratio equation is presented in Figure 2.2.15.
Figure 2.2.15: Relative stiﬀness ratio design chart (Bryson et al., 2011).
2.3
Final Remarks
The authors consulted for the literature review conclude that in general, if good construction
and workmanship is employed, movements around cuts in sands and stiﬀclays are not likely to
be excessive, the system stiﬀness has only marginal inﬂuence on movements in these soils, and
design can consist of ensuring structural adequacy of the supports followed by an estimate of
the ground movements.
Although the results presented by the authors mentioned above provide a useful guide for the
approximation of the maximum deﬂections and settlements induced by deep excavations, most
of the data was obtained from excavations less than 18m in depth with relatively ﬂexible walls
(Peck, 1969) or in soft ground (Clough and O’Rourke, 1990; Bryson et al., 2011). Therefore,
Page 18
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 2. Literature Review
there would be signiﬁcant uncertainties in extrapolating these observations to deeper excava-
tions with stiﬀer walls such as secant pile walls.
Moreover, most of the aforementioned formulas were derived based on either purely clay or
purely sands deposits. The geological description of the Copenhagen soils is, at times, neither
cohesive nor non-cohesive and employing the data produced by the literature presented above
may lead to misleading results.
Page 19
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3
Monitoring Data Interpretation
The objectives of geotechnical instrumentation and monitoring plan involves the analytical as-
sessment of data obtained by instrumentation which may be used to verify the design parameters
and assumptions as well to evaluate the construction technique employed. Moreover, instrumen-
tation data, if properly interpreted, can be used to predict future performance and behaviour in
similar environments (Ahmed, 2014).
The level of instrumentation depends on the variability of site conditions and the category of
the project. The Cityringen follows the criteria for what is considered to be damage as per BRE
Digest 21, ‘Assessment of damage in low-rise buildings, revised 1995’; the acceptable damage for
the project are those that fall under categories 0 and 1 (Eskesen et al., 2014). These categories
involve the hairline cracks of less than about 0.1mm and ﬁne cracks which can be treated easily
using normal decoration (BRE, 1995).
3.1
Raw Data Processing
The construction of the upcoming Cityringen, has been extensively monitored. The monitoring
plan is in place to ensure a safety and to minimize the impact the deep excavation may have
in nearby structures. The permanent instrumentation implemented which is left in the ﬁeld for
more than one year, is as follows:
• Horizontal levelling pins;
• Vertical levelling pins;
• Deep embedment levelling rods:
• 3D Prism Targets and Surface Prisms;
• Inclinometer Casting;
• In-Place Inclinometers (IPI);
• Pillars;
• Automated total stations;
• Total Station Consoles;
• Monitoring wells;
• Crackmeters;
• Load cells.
For the purpose of this thesis, the monitoring data obtained from the in-place inclinometers
(precision: 0.02mm) and load cells is used. All monitoring data is transmitted directly to the
Monitoring Database Kronos and it has been provided for this investigation by the engineers
20
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
at Metroselskabet. During the construction, measurements are recorded every two hours. For
the purpose of this analysis, only two measurements a day are considered which are assumed to
describe the behaviour of the wall and the loads in the struts.
The measurements are extracted from the database given by the CMT team and plotted us-
ing the computer software MATLAB. The graphical representation of the lateral wall movement
during the excavation stages as well for the load in the struts is used with the aim to establish
trend in the data considering the system stiﬀness. The monitoring data of the secant pile walls
is analyzed through empirical and pseudo-ﬁnite element methods in order to describe the actual
wall movements and moment distribution on the wall.
3.2
Empirical Approximation
The monitoring data from the excavations included measurements of displacements and rotations
along the wall. As presented in Zapata-Medina (2007), an expression for the lateral deformation
of the wall can be obtained by ﬁtting a polynomial function to the measured displacements and
rotations of the wall.
A sixth-order polynomial is proposed for the cantilever proﬁle of the deﬂected wall; the
function has the form of,
δh = A1z6 + A2z5 + A3z4 + A4z3 + A5z2 + A6z + A7
(3.2.1)
Where δh is the deﬂection at a given depth and z is the depth along wall.
To ﬁnd the
constants A1 through A7, seven boundary conditions are required. These are deﬁned by the
measured displacements and assumption of moment at the top, which is later described in this
Section.
The systems of equations are set up in matrix form such that the constants in Equation 3.2.1
can be solved as follows,




(3.2.2)
Where the vector

is the constant vector; then,


d
	
(3.2.3)
By substituting the constants found in Equation 3.2.1, the lateral wall movements proﬁle
can be computed.
The bending moment of the wall can be deﬁned from the fundamental relations of mechanics
of material and the classical theory of beams. If the transverse deﬂections, rotations and strains
along the are are so small that the principle of superposition is applicable, then it can be assumed
that the moment and displacement are related as follows:
M = −EI d2y
dx2
(3.2.4)
Similarly, the relationships for the shear force (Equation 3.2.5) and rotation (Equation 3.2.6)
were also proposed by Het´enyi (1971) as:
Page 21
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
Q = −EI d3y
dx3
(3.2.5)
tan θ = −EI dy
dx
(3.2.6)
The bending moment along the wall is then found by diﬀerentiating the polynomial twice
as in Equation 3.2.4. The connection between the retaining wall and the top wall may not be
pinned, and hence assuming a zero moment at the top is not be valid. To approximate the
moment produced at the top of the pile due to the top slab, the structural analysis software
Ftool is used. The software analyses the structural model that consists of the top slab and ﬁxed
supports (Figure 3.2.1), the moment distribution of this system is used for the ﬁxed connection
scenario.
Figure 3.2.1: Ftool Model.
Having the maximum positive moment at the supports, the constants A1 through A7 are
deﬁned with the measured displacements as well for the value for the moment at the top (same
as supports). The two boundary conditions considering the connection at the top slab are then:
• For ‘ﬁxed’ connection:
Mtop = −EI d2y
dx2 = MFtool
(3.2.7)
• For a ‘free’ connection:
Mtop = −EI d2y
dx2 = 0
Furthermore, the retaining walls are deeply embedded in the stiﬀlimestone. Hence,
Mbottom = −EI d2y
dx2 = 0

3.3
System Stiﬀness
A series of well-known eﬀective system stiﬀness parameters have been proposed by Mana et
al. (1981) and Clough, Smith, et al. (1989). However, their design charts were developed using
a limited number of wall types and conﬁguration and the soil conditions which do not described
the soils encountered in Copenhagen. With the purpose of describing the behaviour of the wall
with respect to its stiﬀness and the soil characteristics, the bending stiﬀness proposed by Potts
et al. (1997) is modiﬁed to describe a retaining wall. The bending stiﬀness ρ∗is deﬁned as:
ρ∗= EI/EsH4
(3.3.1)
Where H is the half-width of the beam and Es is representative soil stiﬀness.
3.3.1
Relative Stiﬀness
The relative stiﬀness of the system is deﬁned by making the following changes to Equation 3.3.1:
• EI: bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall as per design;
• Es: stiﬀness of the soil layer at which the maximum deﬂection occur - Eur;
• H: weighted average spacing between supports, havg.
ρ∗= EI/Esh4
avg
(3.3.2)
3.3.2
Relative Equivalent Stiﬀness
The stiﬀness of the soil in contact with the retaining wall inﬂuences the behaviour of the structure
signiﬁcantly, in order to capture this, Equation 3.3.1 shall be modiﬁed as follows:
• EI: bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall as per design;
• Es: equivalent soil stiﬀness, Eseq;
• H: weighted average spacing between supports, havg.
The equivalent soil stiﬀness is deﬁned as the weighted average stiﬀness of the soil layers in
contact with the retaining wall as shown in Figure 3.3.1.
Page 23
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
Figure 3.3.1: Relative equivalent stiﬀness.
ρeq = EI/Eseqh4
avg
(3.3.3)
Where the soil stiﬀness is taken as the unloading-reloading curve, Eur. The displacement
behaviour associated with deep excavations is governed in large by the unload-reload stiﬀness
properties of the soil mass, whether or not the soil was predominantly granular or cohesive
(Storer J. Boone, 2003), EI is the bending stiﬀness of the wall and havg is the average distance
between supports.
3.3.3
Re-deﬁned Relative Equivalent Stiﬀness
The relative stiﬀness between the retaining wall and the soil can be re-deﬁned by taking into
account only the soil in the area of the excavation:
Page 24
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
Figure 3.3.2: Re-deﬁned relative equivalent stiﬀness.
Re-deﬁning the relative equivalent system stiﬀness aims to capture the inﬂuence that the re-
taining soils in contact with the wall solely in the excavation depth have in the overall deﬂection.
The parameter is deﬁned as:
ρeq = EI/Eseq2h4
avg
(3.3.4)
3.4
Pseudo-FE: Frew
Frew (Flexible REtaining Walls) is a program used to output wall embedment, as well as predict
the response of the wall and props in multiple stages. The program allows the user to study the
deformation of, and stresses within, the structure through speciﬁed sequence of construction.
The program calculates displacements, earth pressures, bending moments, shear forces and
support forces occurring during each stage of construction.The program is described herein
following the ‘Frew 19.3: Help Guide’.
3.4.1
Program Features
The main features of Frew are summarized below:
• The geometry of the wall is speciﬁed by a number of nodes which position is expressed as
reduced levels. The representation is shown in Figure 3.4.1:
Page 25
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
Figure 3.4.1: Numerical representation of the Frew model (Oasys, 2017).
• Wall stiﬀness is constant between nodes but may change at nodes. The base of the wall
may be speciﬁed at any node: nodes below this are ‘free’ soil;
• Soil proﬁles are presented by a series of horizontal soil strata that may be diﬀerent at each
side of the wall. The boundaries of soil strata are always located midway node levels;
• Struts may be inserted and removed acting at a node and may have speciﬁed stiﬀness,
pre-stress and lever arm;
• Surcharges may be inserted and removed. Each surcharge consists of uniformly distributed
load or a pressure load of a speciﬁc width;
• Soil may be excavated, backﬁll or changed at each stage at either side of the wall.
• Water pressures may be either hydrostatic or piezometric.
In multi-propped walls, the lowest strut is taken as the rotation strut by the program and
the following assumptions and approximations are made:
• The ground level on the retained side is assumed to be 1cm above the rotation strut;
• Any soil layers above this assumed ground level are treated as equivalent surcharges;
• The ground water distribution is also applied stating from the assumed ground level.
However, the pore pressure from the level of rotation strut downwards are same as the
original pore pressure distribution. The pore pressure from the level assumed ground level
to the level of rotation strut is assumed to vary linearly.
Page 26
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
3.4.1.1
Active and Passive Limits
The active and passive pressures are calculated at the top and base level of each stratum and at
intermediate levels. These are placed where there is a change in linear proﬁle of pressure with
depth. The eﬀective active, p

passive pressure, respectively, while kac and kpc are the cohesive coeﬃcients of active and passive
pressure, respectively. These are evaluated as:
kac = 2

3.4.1.2
Groundwater Flow
Frew allows for the user to specify the groundwater conditions. In the case of the stations in
Cityringen, the groundwater table remains one meter below the excavation, and further excava-
tion proceeds once the GWT has been properly lowered within the excavation pit. To capture
this, the piezometric pressure and the water ﬂow beneath the base is manually input by bal-
ancing the water pressures. The correction for the water pressure is calculated by considering
that the water pressure at the toe of the wall must be the same at the passive and active side of
the wall (shown as uf in Figure 3.4.2). Referring to Figure 3.4.2 below, the piezometric water
pressures are describe below.
The change in head, ∆h, is computed as the ratio between the groundwater levels and the
distance from the active ground water level to the passive, marked with the red lines in Figure
3.4.2.
∆h =
GWLactive −GWLpassive
(GWLactive −Lwall) −(GWLpassive −Lwall)
(3.4.5)
The active and passive water pressure, Pwa and Pwp, acting at a given depth (or node) is
then calculated as:
Pwa = γw(1 −∆h)(GWLactive −z)
(3.4.6)
Pwp = γw(1 + ∆h)(GWLpassive −z)
(3.4.7)
Where z is the distance from the GWLactive to given node or level, shown as z in Figure
3.4.2.

3.4.1.3

The properties for the diﬀerent layers of materials, either side of the wall, are entered in tabular
form. A brief description for each property is presented below:
• E0: Young’s modulus given as a general constant parameter or a speciﬁc value for a given
reference depth;
• γ: bulk unit weight;
• K0: coeﬃcient of earth pressure at rest;
• KaandKp: Earth pressure coeﬃcients: may be calculated or user deﬁned;
• φ

• δ/φ

δ ≤kφ

Page 28
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 3. Monitoring Data Interpretation
Struts and anchors are modelled in terms of an equivalent strut which represents the total
number of struts present in one meter length of wall. For each equivalent strut, the following
items are required:
• The stage number at which the strut is installed;
• The stage number at which the strut is removed;
• Level at which the strut is located;
• Any pre-stressed force applied;
• Stiﬀness deﬁned as:
K = EA/L
Where A is the cross sectional area of the strut, E is the Young’s modulus and L is the
length of the strut.
3.4.2
Full Analysis
The analysis is carried out in steps corresponding to the construction stages of excavation. The
initial stage (Stage 0) is used to calculate the soil stress prior to the installation of the wall.
Here, the displacements are zero.
In the following stages, the incremental displacements due to the changes caused by that
stage are calculated and added to the existing displacements. The soil stresses, strut forces, wall
bending moments and shear forces are then determined.
The wall is modelled as a series of elastic beam elements joined at the nodes. The lowest
node is either the base of the wall or at a prescribed rigid base in the ground beneath the wall.
At each stage of construction the analysis comprises the following steps:
• The initial earth pressures and the out of balance nodal forces are calculated assuming no
moment at the nodes;
• The stiﬀness matrices representing the soil on either side and the wall itself are assembled;
• The soil stiﬀness matrix is combined with the stiﬀness’ representing the actions of struts
or anchors, to form an overall stiﬀness matrix;
• The incremental nodal displacements are calculated from the nodal forces acting on the
overall stiﬀness matrix assuming linear elastic behaviour.
3.4.3
Soil Model
The linear elastic soil model used in Frew is the Safe Method brieﬂy described in the following
paragraphs as presented in the ‘Frew 19.3: Help Guide’.
The method uses a pre-calculated soil stiﬀness matrix; the soil is represented as an elastic
continuum. The method is believed to give good approximations for plane strain situations
where Young’s modulus is constant or increases linearly from zero at the free surface.
Soil is represented as an elastic solid with the soil stiﬀness matrices being developed from
pre-stored stiﬀness matrices calculated using Safe ﬁnite element program. This method is ideal
for soil with linearly increasing stiﬀness with depth.
Page 29
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 4
Stations
4.1
Enghave Plads
Enghave Plads station is constructed in a central public square of the Vesterbro district in
Copenhagen. There are no buildings in the immediate proximity of the site, as shown in the
plan view in Figure 4.1.1 (distance >20m).
Figure 4.1.1: Site plan of Enghave Plads Station, shown framed in blue (taken from Google Earth).
4.1.1
Design Features
The excavation of the station is carried out by a hybrid bottom-up excavation. At the initial
stage of excavation, a top slab is casted to support the secant pile wall, the excavation then
proceeds as a typical bottom-up construction, using two level of struts as temporary supports.
The main design features of the station are summarized as follows (all levels are in m DVR90):
• Ground Level is located at ≈+3m;
• Area of the shaft is approximately 1130m2 (61.5x18.5m);
• Reinforced secant pile walls with a cut oﬀdistance of 23.10m (embedment depth: 4.6m);
• Excavation level within the station at ≈-14.4m.
The typical cross section of the station is shown in Figure 4.1.2.
30
==PAGE:57==

Figure 4.1.2: Typical cross section of Enghave Plads Station (Metroselskabet, 2012b).
4.1.2
Geotechnical Conditions
The results of the soil investigation carried out in the area are presented by the designer in the
‘Enghave Plads - Geotechnical Interpretative Report’ (Metroselskabet, 2012i). The borehole plan
used to investigate the soil conditions are identiﬁed in Figure 4.1.3. The station’s stratigraphy
characteristics can be summarized by the following statements:
• Fill thickness vary from 2 to 5m;
• A 1-5m thick layer of upper clay till is located below the ﬁll layer;
• A 1-3m thick layer of meltwater sand/gravel overlays the limestone;
• The glacially disturbed zone of the limestone is approximately 2.5m thick;
• The transition from UCL to MCL occurs at the level of z=-14.5m, approximately;
• Till deposits may contain cobbles and boulders.

4.1.2.2
Water Levels
As stated by the speciﬁcations in the GIR I and III, the groundwater levels shown in Table 4.1.6
should be applied for the retaining wall design. When analyzing the temporary states (SLS and
ULS) the primary water table is considered, while both the primary and the secondary water

withstand the full hydrostatic water pressure acting behind the walls.
The primary aquifer is mainly composed of Copenhagen limestone and Quarternary melt-

the Greater Copenhagen area. There is also a large number of secondary aquifers, which may
be isolated or in hydraulic connection with each other and with the primary aquifer (Metrosel-
skabet, 2012b).
During the project execution, a number of pumping wells are located in and around the
station box to maintain a dry site, with the GWL remaining at 1m below the excavation level.

to be located at the depth of excavation.

4.1.3
Retaining System Characteristics
Enghave Plads is a ‘shallow’ station with a maximum excavation depth of 18.5m from GL
(z=+3.7m). The retaining system is a secant pile wall, with piles properly overlapped up to a
depth of 23.10m in the main box and 11.50m in the technical room. The piles are 1m in diameter
and spaced at 785mm in the longitudinal sides (parallel to the track) and 800mm spacing along
both transversal sides and the technical room.
4.1.3.1
Permanent Structures
Enghave Plads permanent structure includes the retaining wall, a single-line secant piles wall,
extending up to a depth of 23.1m and a series of permanent slabs, cast after completion.

4.1.4
Construction Sequence
The executive phases of construction of the main box of Enghave Plads were presented in
‘General RW Calculation Report’(Metroselskabet, 2012b) and summarized below:
• Stage 1
Excavation to level z = +3.70m and construction of secant pile walls;
• Stage 2
Excavation to level z = +0.24m and installation of permanent top slab;
• Stage 3
Excavation to level z = −5.65m followed by the installation the of the ﬁrst level of struts
at z = −4.25m;
• Stage 4
Excavation to level z = −10.55m followed by the installation of the second level of struts
at z = −9.15m;
• Stage 5
Final excavation to level z = −14.58m;
• Stage 6
Preparation of the lean concrete level, application of the waterproof membrane and con-
struction of the foundation slab at z = −12.43m;
• Stage 7
Removal of second level of struts and casting of lining walls;
• Stage 8
Installation of temporary columns which support the intermediate and above slabs;
• Stage 9
Removal of temporary bracing at level 1 and realization of Technical Level;
• Stage 10
Demolition of temporary columns, completion of the roof slab and backﬁlling.
The secant piles are installed during the same construction stage, including the lateral boxes.
Moreover, it is ensured that dewatering is maintain to keep the water level 1m below excavation.

the subsequent stage. Because the project focuses in excavation induced movements, Stages 1
through 8 are considered.
4.1.5
Monitoring and Instrumentation Overview
The construction of Enghave Plads is monitored from the beginning of excavation for the top
slab, until a few months after the completion of the station.
Overall, the project focuses on the monitoring data of the secant wall obtained from the
in-place inclinometers, for the horizontal deﬂections of the wall and the monitoring data from
the temporary bracing system from the strain gauges, which reveal information on the forces
acting in the struts. Figure 4.1.7 shows the monitoring plan layout, identifying inclinometers as
EHP-IN## and the load cells as EHP-LC-##.
Page 38
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.1.7: Monitoring layout in Enghave Plads.
4.1.5.1
Inclinometers
A total of nine inclinometers are installed within EHP, as seen in Figure 4.1.7. Two inclinome-
ters are located within the longitudinal walls and one on each of the transversal walls of the
station. The inclinometers are placed within the secant pile and extend up to depths of 17.4m,
19.4m and 26.5m depending on the inclinometer.
Inclinometers 27 and 28 are installed within the technical room, which is built after comple-
tion of the main station. At this location, the deﬂections of the wall during Stages 1 through 8
are minimal and not comparable to the main box deformation. The deformation behaviour at
the central section of the shorter sides, aﬀected by concrete arching eﬀect, is three-dimensional
and the displacement should be much smaller (Ou, 2006) and because the project focuses on the
maximum displacements, only the inclinometers on the long side of the main box are interpreted,
assuming two-dimensional plane strain behaviour (21, 22, 24 and 25).
The following summarizes the characteristics of each inclinometers:
• INC 21
The inclinometer is installed up to a depth of 19.4m, 3.7m above the toe of the wall; the
ﬁxity of the inclinometer cannot be conﬁrmed nor assumed. There is no data available for
Stage 1 and Stage 17. It is located on the North wall, within the border of the technical
room.
• INC 22
The inclinometer is installed up to a depth of 19.4m, 3.7m above the toe of the wall; the
ﬁxity of the inclinometer cannot be conﬁrmed nor assumed. There is no data available for
Stage 1. It is located on the North wall, within the border of the technical room.
• INC 24
The inclinometer is installed up to a depth of 25.4m, 2.3m below the toe of the wall, as-
Page 39
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 4. Stations
sumed to be ﬁxed at this location. There is no data available for Stage 1. The inclinometer
is considered ‘free-ﬁeld’ due to its distance to any structure.
• INC 25
The inclinometer is installed up to a depth of 25.4m, 2.3m below the toe of the wall,
assumed to be ﬁxed at this location. There is no data available for Stage 1 and Stage
6.
The inclinometer is considered ‘free-ﬁeld’.
EHP-IN25 showed malfunctions during
construction, and only recorded measurements four times a month. The inclinometer was
later decommissioned, hence its measurements are neither reliable nor representative.
There is no data corresponding to the ﬁrst stage for all inclinometers, regardless, after a
preliminary assessment, the inclinometers present reliable information. Figure 4.1.8 shows the
inclinometer distance from the temporary bracing system.
Figure 4.1.8: Distance from inclinometers to struts.
4.1.5.2
Strain Gauges
As a general approach, the strut loads are measured with three sets of strain gauges on each side
of the selected struts. The CMT designers chose critical struts to be monitored which experience
the highest loads in the design, and are deemed appropriate to understand the overall behaviour
of the structure. The struts are located at axis C, F H and K as seen in Figure 4.1.9. The
unmonitored struts are not expected to be in a worse condition than the monitored ones.

Based on the construction timeline presented in Table 4.1.9, the cumulative displacements of
the secant wall through the construction stages are obtained for each of the inclinometers. The
lateral movements occurring during construction at the diﬀerent elevations (with the correspond-
ing soil type) are shown in Figure 4.1.10 for EHP-IN24. Additional time elapses are found in
Appendix A.3.
Note that negative displacements (outward movement) correspond to a movement of the wall
towards the retained soil while positive displacements (inward movement) refer to the movements
of the wall towards the excavation.

Page 41
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 4. Stations
The construction process is grouped into eight main stages and ends at Stage 8: Preparation
of concrete level. Once the ﬁrst excavation is completed, and the casting of the top slab starts,
lateral movements at Levels 0.4-7.4m gradually increase towards the retained soil. During the
second excavation, there is a change in direction of wall moment outwards at depths 4.4m and
7.4m, and remains throughout the construction of the station.
The rate of lateral moment remains relatively constant during Stages 3 and 4. During the
excavations of the ML2 and UCL I layer at Stage 5: excavation for 2nd level of struts, an increase
in lateral movement is recorded at all depths, however the change at shallow depths (0.4-1.4m)
is less drastic. An increase in rate of movement is also recorded during Stage 6: installation
of2nd level of struts. Towards completion of the excavation, the rate of lateral moment is steady
at around 0.01mm/day. The maximum rate and lateral movement were observed at level 10.4m
at the ML2 soil layer.
A similar trend at depths 4.4m-19.4m are seen in EHP-IN21 and EHP-IN22. After comparing
the measurements of the three inclinometers, the following is established:
• Rate of lateral wall movement is minimal during Stages 4 and 5, having an increase of
δh < 0.5mm. Similarly, the rate remains steady and minimal at 0.01mm/day during Stage
7 and 8;
• Increase in lateral wall movement occur during Stage 5 and 6 which involve the excavation
and installation of the second level of struts;
• EHP-IN21 and EHP-IN22 show an outward movement at shallow depths (0.4-1.4m),
whereas EHP-IN22 show little displacement until Stage 5, during which the wall has an
inward movement;
• The largest displacements are located at a depth of 10.4m within the UCL I layer.
The increase in displacement during Stage 5 for all inclinometers may be owed to the large
unsupported excavation during this time. At this time, the average support spacing is havg =
6.7m, which is the largest in the construction of Enghave Plads.
4.1.6.1
Lateral Wall Movements
The lateral wall movements measured at EHP-IN24 during construction are presented in Figure
4.1.11. Additional lateral deformation proﬁles are provided in Appendix A.3.
Throughout the construction stages, the lateral deformations at EHP-IN24 is largely re-
strained at the top by the slab. In late construction stages, the ﬁeld data shows that the wall
has a deep inward movement and that the maximum lateral deformations occur near the exca-
vation surface, corresponding to the deformation behaviour of a wall supported by high stiﬀness
struts (Ou, 2006); similar behaviour is reported by EHP-IN21 and EHP-IN22, as seen in Ap-
pendix A.3. The occurrence of soft material at shallow depths and presence of the top slab which
has quite high axial stiﬀness may also contribute to this deformation pattern. The following
summarizes in part the observations:
• A rotation point is observed below the excavation depth from Stage 3 onwards;
• An outward cantilever shape is observed during Stage 2 which may be attributed to the
calibration of the inclinometer and this behaviour may not be representative;
• The locations of the holes in the slab with respect to the inclinometers may aﬀect the
displacement of the head of the piles but not information regarding this.
Page 42
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.1.11: Lateral wall movements at EHP-IN24. Dashed red lines represent the excavation depth,
black dashed lines, the struts and thick black line shows the location of the top slab.
The deformed shapes at each location at the end of each stage was investigated. Figure
4.1.12 presents the deformed shape at the end of Stage 8: preparation of concrete level. At
this time, the maximum and minimum displacements were recorded by EHP-IN22 and EHP-25,
respectively. EHP-21 and 22 are placed the closest to struts which may explain the lower dis-
placements at these locations.
The retaining wall shows a point of rotation just below the excavation depth for EHP-IN25.
This is an expected behaviour for retaining walls to have a point of rotation somewhere along
the embedment depth.
Most of the maximum displacements for each inclinometer occur at the same depth with
similar magnitude. However, during Stage 3 and 4, EHP-IN24 shows large outward movements
and the maximum displacement is recorded close to the head of the wall in the soft ﬁll layer
(FY).

of a strut during which obvious ‘jumps’ were observed. The relative displacements between an
installation and an excavation stage is smaller possibly due to struts introducing a more abrupt
lateral pressure than earth pressure does, so the removal of soil leads to a smooth change in
displacement in this case; however, this does not explain the sudden increase in displacement
in Stage 5. Lastly, the increase in displacement during Stage 8 may be attributed to the re-
laxation of the system stiﬀness and the delay of the construction of the top slab which may
induce additional ground movements: there are around 3 months prior to any casting activity
(Hsiung, 2009).

4.1.6.3
Bending Moment Distribution
Enghave Plads has a ﬁxed connection between the wall and the piles.
The Ftool model is
presented in Figure 4.1.13, the uniform distributed load is as speciﬁed in the ‘Detailed Desgin
1a RW- Enghave Plads’ Metroselskabet (2012b) with a self-weight of 7.72kPa and variable loads
of 15kPa.
Figure 4.1.13:
Ftool model for Enghave Plads, the maximum positive moment at the supports is
648kNm.
The moment distribution is obtained by diﬀerentiating twice the polynomial function sim-
ulating the deformation curve, as discussed in Section 3.2. The deﬂected shape measured in
Enghave Plads is presented in Figure 4.1.14 and the bending moment distribution in Figure
4.1.15, assuming two diﬀerent connections.
Page 45
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.1.14: Sixth-order polynomial function for the end of Stage 8.
(a) Pinned connection
(b) Fixed connection
Figure 4.1.15: Bending Moment Distribution.
The largest moment occurs at the head of the secant pile, as proposed in the design of the
retaining wall in Metroselskabet (2012b). It is know that a ‘ﬁxed’ connection is never fully ﬁxed,
and there will be some moment that is to be carried by the slab. The behaviour of the wall is
somewhere in between a pinned and a fully ﬁxed connections.
For a pinned connection, the maximum negative and positive bending moment are ≈-
310kNm/m at z=-21.8m and ≈250kNm/m at z=-10.7m, respectively. The fully ﬁxed connection
Page 46
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 4. Stations
yields maximum bending moments of ≈-316kNm/m at z=-22.0m and ≈215kNm/m at z=-11.4m.
The maximum moment presented by the designer at the SLS in the ‘Detailed Desgin 1a RW-
Enghave Plads’ at the head of the wall is ≈-1800kNm/m well above than those estimated. This
correlates to the overestimation of displacements.
4.1.6.4
Strut Performance
The loads measured at EHP-LC-F (axis K) in Figures 4.1.7 and Figures 4.1.9 are shown in
Figure 4.1.16. Inclinometers EHP-IN24 and EHP-IN22 are in proximity of the load cell and will
be discussed hereon.
An steady increase in load is observed in Strut 1, during the excavation for 2nd level of
struts; there is also a clear increase in displacement at all depths during the Stage 6, due to the
installation of the 2nd level of struts. The peak force of the struts, 1500kN and 1000kN for Strut 1
and 2, respectively, are reached as the horizontal displacements stabilize during Stage 7, towards
the end of the ﬁnal excavation. No other information regarding the struts has been available.
Further investigation on this matter may include the eﬀect of pre-load on displacements.
Figure 4.1.16: Strut Load changes during construction - EHP-LC-F.
4.1.6.5
Discussion of Performance
The ﬁnal construction stages, after casting the slabs, removing struts and breaking of the tracks
have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the ﬁnal lateral maximum displacement. Figure 4.1.17 depicts
the maximum lateral deformation relative to the maximum lateral deformation at completion.
The lateral movement at the end of Stage 8 accounts for 58% of the total lateral movement of
the secant wall. The maximum displacement occurs during Stage 16: Break out Track 2; the
abrupt changes in Figure 4.1.17 correspond to the removal of a strut or the breaking of a track.
These actions cause a sudden alteration to the in situ stresses rather than a gradual change
in stress, as the excavation does. This should be considered when assessing the calculation of
maximum displacements and the design of the reinforcement of the retaining wall.

The observed performance in terms of the maximum lateral wall movements,δhmax, as the
excavation progresses is shown in Figure 4.1.18. The upper and lower bounds correspond to
EHP-IN22 and EHP-IN24, respectively.
The results are discussed in relation to the empirical correlations mentioned in Chapter 2.
The horizontal moment to excavation depth ratio (δh/He) reported by Clough and O’Rourke (1990)
ranged from 0.2 to 0.3% for excavations in stiﬀsoils using stiﬀsupport systems such as diaphragm
walls. Enghave Plads presents δh/He ratios between 0.015% to 0.02% if only excavation activity
is considered and these are among the smallest in the Cityringen. There is relatively not much
increase in the maximum displacement with respect to excavation depth.
Figure 4.1.18: Observed maximum lateral wall deformations with respect to excavation depth.
The system stiﬀness and the lateral wall movements are studied for EHP-IN21, 22 and 24, as
Page 48
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 4. Stations
per discussed in Section 3.3, these are shown in Figures 4.1.19 and 4.1.20. The upper bound of
the data set corresponds to EHP-IN22, the lower bound corresponds to EHP-IN21. The system
stiﬀness is deﬁned by the following:
• EI: bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall as per design:
EI=1977MPa;
• Es: stiﬀness of the soil layer at which the maximum deﬂection occur - Eur from Table
4.1.4;
• Eseq: equivalent soil stiﬀness as per deﬁned in Chapter 3, Section 3.3:
Eseq= 1400MPa;
• havg: weighted average spacing between supports at any given stage.
There is a large scatter in the data when using the parameter of the relative stiﬀness, ρ∗, while
when interpreting the data with respect to the relative stiﬀness using the Equivalent Stiﬀness,
ρef, a growing trend is observed. Smaller displacements are observed at all locations during
Stage 4 - Installation of ﬁrst level of struts; the introduction of the support system prevents
excessive lateral movement, but as the system stiﬀness reduces due to the excavation height, the
displacement increase, as seen for Stages 5 through 8. However, there is large scattering in the
data and a general trend cannot be speciﬁed.
Figure 4.1.19: Relative Stiﬀness,ρ∗, compared to normalized displacements.

Figure 4.1.20: Relative Equivalent Stiﬀness, ρeq, compared to normalized displacements.
4.1.7
Summary and Conclusion
Enghave Plads is a relatively shallow excavation (Hex=18.5m) supported by two levels of struts
and secant piles with a length extending up to 23.1m. Reliable data was available for 3 incli-
nometers, two of which were located along a wall within a technical room (EHP-IN21 and 22)
and one (EHP-IN24) was considered ‘free-ﬁeld’. The following summarizes the ﬁndings at EHP
for the site and the inclinometers mentioned:
• The Upper Copenhagen Limestone is encountered at a relatively shallow depth (z=-7.5m)
- the occurrence of stiﬀsoil/rock increases signiﬁcantly the relative equivalent stiﬀness of
the system;
• The overall maximum displacement δhmax at the end of excavation for all three inclinome-
ters ranged between 3.2-4.1mm;
• The δhmax/Hex ratio varies between 0.015-0.02Hex%;
• δhmax was located within the UCL I layer, at approximately 0.4Hex.
The expected movements at EHP-IN21 and 22 were expected to be signiﬁcantly smaller to
those experienced in EHP-IN24, but the behaviour of the wall at all locations was similar. This
can be attributed to the fact that the length of the retaining wall in the main box is enough for
its active wedge not to interact with the walls in the technical room having no inﬂuence in the
system stiﬀness.
The displacements were not heavily inﬂuenced by the system stiﬀness, however a slight
increasing normalized ratio with increasing system stiﬀness is observed. This is may be owed to
the occurrence of stiﬀmaterial at a relatively shallow depth, z=-7.5m as seen in Figure 4.1.11
and the presence of the relatively stiﬀslab at the head of the piles.
Page 50
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 4. Stations
4.2
Frederiksberg
Frederiksberg Station is located within the district it is named after. It is currently operational
for the metro lines M1 and M2. The upcoming M3 line crosses below the existing M1 and M2
lines, seen in Figure 1.1.1. The station area is constrained by residential buildings located in
the East and to the West, by the Frederiksberg Gymnasium. The shopping mall Frederiksberg
Center is located to the North of the station, as seen in Figure 4.2.1.
Figure 4.2.1: Plan view of Frederiksberg Station, shown framed by blue lines (taken from Google Earth).
4.2.1
Design Features
Frederiksberg Station is a deep and wide underground excavation, with a width of 18.5m between
lining walls.
The station is supported by secant piles extending with length of 26m.
The
excavation is carried out by a modiﬁed bottom-up construction method, with permanent slabs
and three level of braces as temporary support. The designer present the main site and design
features as follows (Metroselskabet, 2012l) (all levels are in m DVR90):
• Ground level is at approximately z=+11.5m;
• Area of the station is ≈1400m2 (18.5x75.6m);
• Cut oﬀlevel for the retaining walls at level z=-17.6m (embedment depth: 2.4m);
• Excavation level within the station at z=-11.9m;
• Main staircase to concourse level at z=+5.3m is located in the south end of the station.
• Connection to the existing metro station is located in the north end of the station.
The longitudinal section of the station is shown in Figure 4.2.2, Figure 4.2.3 shows the typical
cross section.
Page 51
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.2.2: Longitudinal section of Frederiksberg Station (Metroselskabet, 2012c).
Figure 4.2.3: Typical cross section of Frederiksberg Station (Metroselskabet, 2012c).
4.2.2
Geotechnical Conditions
The soil investigation and interpretation for Frederiksberg are presented by the designer in
‘Frederiksberg - Geotechnical Design Report’ (Metroselskabet, 2012l).
The site stratigraphy
characteristics are summarized with following statements:
• Fill thickness varies from 3 to 4m;
• A 5-9m thick layer of upper clay till (ML1) is located below the ﬁll layer;
• A 1-3.5m thick layer of meltwater sand/gravel overlays the limestone;
• The glacially disturbed zone of the limestone is approximately 0.5-1.0m thick;
• The transition from UCL to MCL occurs at the level of z=-10m;
• Till deposits may contain cobbles and boulders
The borehole plan for site investigation is presented in Figure 4.2.4, identiﬁed by blue and red
marks. Based on this, the ground conditions at the construction site proposed by the designer
is presented in Table 4.2.1. The stratigraphy is shown in Figure 4.2.5. For the identiﬁcation
Page 52
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 4. Stations
of the individual layers, geological description, classiﬁcation tests and results of laboratory and
geotechnical/geophysical in situ tests were employed.
Figure 4.2.4: Borehole plan for Frederiksberg (Metroselskabet, 2012l).
Table 4.2.1: Soil and limestone at Frederiksberg Station (Metroselskabet, 2012l).
Page 53
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.2.5: Layering of soil and limestone at Frederiksberg Station (Metroselskabet, 2012l).
Seven geotechnical borings revealed a relatively consistent homogeneous soil proﬁle. The ﬁll
layer underlays a layer of upper clay till followed by a thin layer of upper meltwater sand/gravel.
The Copenhagen Limestone is overlaid by lower clay/sand till; the excavation level is located
within this unit as well for the tip of the secant walls. Further detailed geological description
can be found in Appendix C.1.
4.2.2.1
Design Proﬁles
The geological borings revealed relatively consistent homogeneous soil layers. The most critical
design proﬁles proposed in the GIR Level III are presented in Table 4.2.2.
The designer has chosen Proﬁle 1 and 2 to be the most critical. This proﬁle was chosen after
six SLS models were carried out to evaluate the variation of the bending moment and the lateral
wall displacements: the chosen proﬁle yielded the highest moments and displacements. The
characteristic values used for the design of the secant pile wall are shown in Table 4.2.3 where
Emax and Gmax refer to the initial small strain modulus while Evc and Eur correspond to the
design elastic modulus used for the modeling and design of the retaining wall. A justiﬁcation for
the chosen characteristic parameters is presented in Appendix C.2. The parameters have been
deemed conservative by the designer.
Page 54
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 4. Stations
Table 4.2.2: Design Proﬁles at Frederiksberg Station (Metroselskabet, 2012c).
Table 4.2.3: Geotechnical design values (Metroselskabet, 2012c).
4.2.2.2
Water Levels
As stated by the speciﬁcations in GIR I and III, the groundwater levels shown in Table 4.2.4
should be applied for the retaining wall design.
Table 4.2.4: Water levels at Frederiksberg station (Metroselskabet, 2012c).
The primary water table is considered for the SLS and ULS analyses due to the proximity
of the secondary water table. For the ALS analysis, the piezometric head in the primary and
secondary aquifer is considered to ensure a safe working place and the stability of structures.
The retaining structures are designed to withstand the full hydrostatic water pressure acting
behind the walls.
The primary aquifer is mainly composed of Copenhagen limestone and Quarternary melt-
water sand and gravel in hydraulic connection with the limestone and has a regional extent in
the Greater Copenhagen area. There is also a large number of secondary aquifers, which may
Page 55
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 4. Stations
be isolated or in hydraulic connection with each other and with the primary aquifer (Metrosel-
skabet, 2012c).
During the project execution, a number of pumping wells are located in and around the sta-
tion box to maintain a dry site, with the GWL remaining at 1m below excavation level. After
completion and removal of temporary support system, the groundwater level is considered to be
located at the depth of excavation.
4.2.3
Retaining System Characteristics
Frederiksberg is considered a ‘deep’ station with a maximum excavation depth of 23.0m from
GL (z=+11.5m). The plan view of Frederiksberg is shown in Figure 4.2.6.
Figure 4.2.6: Plan view of Frederiksberg Station (Metroselskabet, 2012c).
4.2.3.1
Permanent Structures
Frederiksberg permanent structures include the retaining wall (double and single-line) and a
series of permanent slabs, cast after completion.
Retaining Wall System
The station is surrounded by two buildings: Frederiksberg Gymnasium (founded at z=+1.03m)
to the West and a residential building to the East (founded at z=+8.23m). In order to prevent
any excessive deformation that may lead to structural damage in the existing infrastructure,
double secant piles have been installed within these locations, elsewhere the wall is constructed
by means of single secant piles overlapped for depths up to 26.0m. The characteristics of the
continuous diaphragm wall along the building are shown in Table 4.2.5. Figure 4.2.7 shows the
station and the wall map.

Permanent Slabs
The permanent structural elements within the station serve as permanent support for the wall.
In the permanent stage, only the roof slab will be connected to the retaining walls and is allowed
to act in tension/compression. The thickness of the structures are as follows:
• Roof Slab: varies with an equivalent area of Aeq = 0.61m2/m;
• Concourse Slab: 0.62m;
• Intermediate Slab: 1.80m;
• Bottom Slab: max. 1.80m;
• Lining wall: 0.85m.
4.2.3.2
Temporary Support Systems
The temporary steel struts are designed to prevent any excessive settlement during construction
and are kept in compression by means of hydraulic jacks during the construction phase. The
characteristics of the steel pipes used during the construction of the station are summarized
in Table 4.2.6. A plan view for the strut layout is presented in Figure 4.2.8. All struts are
prestressed at 550kN after installation. The robustness of the strut system has been assessed
and deemed suﬃcient to provide redundancy of the retaining wall/strut system and is able to
withstand the loss of one critical element without resulting in an incremental collapse mechanism
(Metroselskabet, 2012c).

4.2.4
Construction Sequence
The executive phases of construction of the main box of Frederiksberg were presented in ‘General
RW Calculation Report’(Metroselskabet, 2012c) and summarized below:
• Stage 1
Installation of retaining wall;
• Stage 2
Excavation to level z = +8.35m followed by the casting of the top slab between axis C
and K (Figure 4.2.7) at level z = +10.35m;
• Stage 3
Excavation to level z = 1.00m followed by the installation of the 1st level of struts at level
z = +2.50m;
• Stage 4
Excavation to level z = −4.00m followed by the installation of the 2nd level of struts at
z = −2.5m;
• Stage 5
Excavation to level z = −8.50m followed by the installation of the 3rd level of struts at
z = −7.00m;
• Stage 6
Excavation to underside of base slab at level z = −11.97m followed by the casting of the
base slab at z = −10.6m0;
Page 58
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 4. Stations
• Stage 7
Removal of third level of struts;
• Stage 8
Removal of second level of struts;
• Stage 9
Execution of lining wall from base slab to underside of intermediate slab;
• Stage 10
Installation of temporary steel structure which supports the intermediate slabs and the
ones above, until the end of the construction of the internal structures. The intermediate
slab is installed at level z = −2.22m;
• Stage 11
Construction of lining walls between intermediate level of slab and underside of technical
level slab at z = −0.22m. Construction of the technical level slab as well for the installation
of temporary bracing at level z = −0.20m between the axis C and K shown in Figure 4.2.7;
• Stage 12
Removal of ﬁrst level of struts and construction of lining walls between technical level slab
and underside of technical level slab at level z = −0.22m only in the portion between axis
C and K in Figure 4.2.7.
During construction, it is ensured that dewatering is maintain to keep the water level 1m
below excavation.

The start date of the installation of the struts corresponds to the day the monitoring software
