4.5.6.3
Bending Moment Distribution
At Nørrebros Runddel, the connection between the retaining walls and the top slab was ﬁxed
temporarily and pinned permanently through the pile concrete cover. However, for the purpose
of this analysis both connections are investigated. The structural model used in Ftool is shown
in Figure 4.5.17 and the material properties and the loads are as prescribed by the designer and
summarized in Section 4.5.3.1. The distributed load includes the self-weight of the structure
(18.25kPa) and temporary variable loads (15kPa).
Figure 4.5.17: Ftool model for Nørrebros Runddel, with a moment at the ends of 958.6kNm.
The lateral wall deformation is presented in Figure 4.5.18: the polynomial slightly over-
Page 133
==PAGE:160==
Chapter 4. Stations
estimates the displacements below z=-17.4m by less than 2mm which does not have a signiﬁcant
inﬂuence to the approximation of the bending moment. Following the methodology presented
in Section 3.2 the moment distributions for the pinned and ﬁxed conditions are computed and
presented in Figure 4.5.19.
A ‘ﬁxed’ connection is never truly fully ﬁxed, and there will be some moment that is to be
carried by the slab and part of it to be transferred to the pile and redistributed in the pile cap.
The behaviour of the wall is then somewhere in between a pinned and a fully ﬁxed connections
presented below.
For a pinned connection, the maximum negative and positive bending moment are ≈-
767kNm/m at z=-28.4m and ≈906kNm/m at z=-15.3m, respectively; the fully ﬁxed connec-
tion yields maximum bending moments within the wall of ≈-826kNm/m at z=-28.7m and
≈932kNm/m at z=-15.8m.
The moments obtained by this approximation follow a diﬀerent
distribution and are smaller than those in the design (Metroselskabet, 2012d).
Figure 4.5.18: Sixth-order polynomial function for the end of Stage 12.
Page 134
==PAGE:161==
Chapter 4. Stations
(a) Pinned connection
(b) Fixed connection
Figure 4.5.19: Bending Moment Distribution.
4.5.6.4
Strut Performance
The loads from strut A (axis C) and B (axis D) from Figure 4.5.12 are illustrated in Figure
4.5.20 and 4.5.21. NOR-IN27 is in proximity of this strut, and behaviour is hereon compared
to it. The installation of the second level of struts produce an increase in load in the ﬁrst level;
during this time, the lateral movement of the wall stabilize and there is no obvious increase in
deﬂections. Moreover, the load in Strut 1 remains stable.
Strut 3 (z=-10.6m) experiences an increase in load in early May 2013. This date corresponds
to Stage 9: Excavation for fourth level of struts during which there is also a clear increase in
rate of displacement of the wall, specially at a depth of 17.4m, close to the excavation depth.
The maximum wall displacement throughout the excavation stages are recorded at levels 11.4m
and 14.4m corresponding to the DS/DG soil unit, this coincides well with the maximum load
on Strut 3 of approximately 3500kN, located at a depth of 10.6m.

The lateral wall movements are investigated up to what the designer deﬁnes as ´completion’
or the time prior to the casting of the bottom slab. However, the ﬁnal construction stages (i.e
Page 136

Chapter 4. Stations
casting of slabs, removal of support system, breaking of tracks) have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on
the ﬁnal lateral maximum displacement. Figure 4.5.22 presents the maximum lateral movement
relative to the maximum lateral deformation at the end of the project. The maximum lateral
movement occurs during Stage 21: Construction of Technical Level at a depth of 14.4m. The
lateral movement at the end of Stage 12 accounts for the 80% of the maximum movement.

Figure 4.5.22: Lateral deformation behaviour relative to construction progress.
The maximum lateral displacement of each stage and the excavation depth can be discussed
in term of that presented in Chapter 2; these are shown in Figure 4.5.23.
The upper and
lower bounds correspond to NOR-IN25 and NOR-IN27. Similar relationships of δhmax/He ratio
reported by Clough and O’Rourke (1990) ranged between 0.2% and 0.3%. Although the ratios
found for Nørrebros Runddel are the highest among the Cityringen, they are still much less
than those previously reported. The case histories on which Clough and O’Rourke (1990) based
their results considered a wide range of soil conditions, focusing solely in purely cohesive or
non-cohesive soils, excavation support and follows diﬀerent construction sequence, all of which
aﬀect the magnitude of the displacements and the relationships should be taken only as reference
values.
Page 137
==PAGE:164==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.5.23: Observed maximum lateral wall deformations with respect to excavation depth.
The system stiﬀness and the lateral wall movements are studied for NOR-IN23, 26 and 27,
shown in Figure 4.5.24 and 4.5.25. The system stiﬀness is deﬁned by the following:
• EI: bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall as per design:
EI=2025MPa;
• Es: stiﬀness of the soil layer at which the maximum deﬂection occur - Eur from Table
4.5.4;
• Eseq: equivalent soil stiﬀness as per deﬁned in Chapter 3, Section 3.3:
Eseq= 313MPa;
• havg: weighted average spacing between supports at any given stage.
The upper bound of the data set corresponds to NOR-IN25, the lower bound corresponds
to NOR-IN23. The data shows an evident trend - as the relative stiﬀness increases, so does
the displacement. For Nørrebros Runddel, high relative stiﬀness is due to small havg and soft
ground encountered on site. During Stage 4, the havg and He are small but the displacements
are similar in magnitude to the subsequent stages (see Tables 4.5.8 and 4.5.9), this combination
may explain the discrepancy between these points and the rest of the data. It is possible that if
more data was available for previous stages, these would have plotted close to Stage 4.
Page 138
==PAGE:165==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.5.24: Relative Stiﬀness compared to normalized displacements.
Figure 4.5.25: Relative Equivalent Stiﬀness compared to normalized displacements.
4.5.8
Summary and Conclusions
Nørrebros Runddel is a deep excavation that has a depth of 24.4m and supported by walls
up to 28.3m long. The top slab is casted in the central part of the excavation and struts are
positioned at the edges. The station is then supported by 4 levels of struts at the exterior and
3 levels within the interior of the main excavation, below the slab. The stratigraphy of the site
is dominated by the middle meltwater sand/gravel which inﬂuences the relative stiﬀness of the
system. The main results are summarized below:
• The Upper Copenhagen Limestone is encountered at a depth of z=-19.8m;
• The overall maximum displacement δhmax at the end of excavations ranged between 21.7-
26.8mm;
Page 139
==PAGE:166==
Chapter 4. Stations
• The δhmax/Hex ratio varies between 0.07-0.122Hex%;
• δhmax was located within the DS/DG layer, at approximately 0.6Hex.
Nørrebros Runddel station presented the largest displacements of all stations. NOR-IN25 and
27 were just above the calculated measurements for the retaining wall. The large displacements
at NOR-IN25 may be explained by the installation of the retaining wall in the emergency exit hall
and having no support at the pile head. The large displacements within the station in comparison
to other location in the Cityringen may be due to the relatively soft ground encountered at the
site and the lack of stiﬀmaterial such as UCL.
Page 140
==PAGE:167==
Chapter 4. Stations
4.6
Vibenshus Runddle
The present section involves the data interpretation for Vibenshus Runddel station. The sta-
tion is located on the corner between Jagtvej and Nørre Alle and has no signiﬁcant structures
surrounding the excavation, as seen in Figure 4.6.1.
Figure 4.6.1: Site plan of Vibenshus Runddel, shown in blue lines (taken from Google Earth).
4.6.1
Design Features
Vibenshus Runddel station is a shallow underground station with a width of 18.5m between
lining walls and a length of 63.2m between head walls. The excavation is carried out by a hybrid
bottom-up method with two permanent slabs (top and bottom) and two levels of struts. The
main design features are given as follows (levels shown as DVR90):
• Ground level varies from approximately +13.3m to +12.7m;
• Area of the station is approximately 1250m2;
• Secant pile wall length up to 36m (embedment depth: 17.2m);
• Excavation level within station level, z=-5.9m (He = 18.6m);
• Concourse level is at z=+6.2;
• Two technical rooms are located at the southern and eastern side of the station.
4.6.2
Geotechnical Conditions
The identiﬁcation of the individual layers has been based on the geological description in com-
bination with the classiﬁcation tests and the results of the geophysical tests carried out in situ.
The borehole plan is shown in Figure 4.6.2.
The geotechnical conditions for the site are summarized in the ‘Geotechnical Design and
Interpretative GIR III Reports’ (Metroselskabet, 2014f). The ground conditions at the con-
struction site are summarized in Table 4.6.1 and Figure 4.6.3.
Page 141
==PAGE:168==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.6.2: Borehole plan - Vibenshus Runddel (Metroselskabet, 2014f).
Table 4.6.1: Soil and limestone at Vibenshus Runddel (Metroselskabet, 2014f).
Page 142
==PAGE:169==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.6.3: Layering of soil and limestone (Metroselskabet, 2014f).
As seen in Figure 4.6.3, the soil stratigraphy has little variation. The occurrence of the
meltwater and interstadial clay units (DL2) and the lower clay till unit (ML3) is very sporadic
and only located in boring P09.100. Appendix B.1 presents a detail description of the geological
description of the soil layers encountered in Vibenshus Runddel.
4.6.2.1
Design Proﬁles
The geological borings revealed relatively consistent homogeneous soil layers. The most critical
design proﬁles proposed in the GIR III are presented in Table 4.6.2. The designer has chosen
Proﬁle 5 and 6 for the design of the secant piles, since these generate the highest values of
bending moments in piles (Metroselskabet, 2014b). The values given in Table 4.6.3 summarize
the main geotechnical design parameters used in the design of the secant piles and a justiﬁcation
for the chosen parameters is presented in Appendix B.2.
Page 143
==PAGE:170==
Chapter 4. Stations
Table 4.6.2: Design Proﬁles for Vibenshus Runddel (Metroselskabet, 2014g).
Table 4.6.3: Design Proﬁles for Vibenshus Runddel (Metroselskabet, 2014b).
4.6.2.2
Water Levels
The design groundwater has been deﬁned in accordance to the GIR III, together with the local
soil conditions. The groundwater levels applied to the design are listed in Table 4.6.4. The
primary water table is considered when analyzing the temporary states (SLS and ULS), whereas
both the primary and the secondary water levels are considered for the permanent state design.
The primary aquifer is mainly composed of Copenhagen limestone and Quarternary melt-
water sand and gravel in hydraulic connection with the limestone and has a regional extent in
the Greater Copenhagen area. There is also a large number of secondary aquifer, which may be
isolated or in hydraulic connection with each other and with the primary aquifer (Metroselska-
bet, 2014b).
During the project execution, a number of pumping wells are located in and around the
excavation to maintain a dry site, with the GWL remaining at 1m below excavation level. The
retaining structures are designed to withstand the full hydrostatic water pressure acting behind
the walls.
Page 144
==PAGE:171==
Chapter 4. Stations
Table 4.6.4: Design groundwater tables at Vibenshus Runddel (Metroselskabet, 2014g)
4.6.3
Retaining System Characteristics
Vibenshus Runddel is a shallow station with a maximum excavation depth equal to 18.55m.
The retaining wall consists of secant piles, properly overlapped for depths up to 36.0m in the
main box (with no reinforcement in the last 8m of wall) and 13.8m for the area of the technical
room. The retaining structures are 1.0m diameter secant piles at 800, 786 and 750mm horizontal
spacing (Metroselskabet, 2014b).
4.6.3.1
Permanent Structures
The permanent structures in Vibenshus Runddel involve the retaining wall which is a single-line
secant piles extending up to a maximum depth of 36m, with the head of the wall located at
z = +11m and the toe at z = −5.57m, and two a series of permanent slabs, cast after comple-
tion. The top slab is ﬁrst casted (top-bottom), with the excavation level at around 2m below
the slab. Excavation proceeds and the installation of two level of struts to provide temporary
support to the wall (bottom-up).
Retaining Wall System
The properties of the secant piles are summarized below and are based on the ‘General Retaining
Wall Calculation Report’ (Metroselskabet, 2014b):

4.6.3.2
Temporary Support Systems
The excavation at Vibenshus Runddel is supported by two level of struts, and 10 steel struts
distributed along the length of the station. The temporary steel struts are designed to prevent
any excessive settlement during construction and are kept in compression by means of hydraulic
jacks during the construction phase. The characteristics of the steel pipes are summarized in
Table 4.6.6. A prestress force of 550kN is applied to each strut after installation.

4.6.4
Construction Sequence
The excavation phases followed for the construction of Vibenshus Runddel Station are presented
in the ‘General Retaining Wall Calculation Report’, are summarized below:
• Stage 1
Excavation to level z = +11.0m from the average ground level of z = +12.9m and pro-
ceeded by the installation of the secant pile walls;
• Stage 2
Excavation to level z = +9.25m and casting of the top slab at level z = +10.25m to
provide support for the retaining walls;
• Stage 3
Excavation to level z = +2.50m followed by the installation of the ﬁrst level of temporary
bracing at z = +4.40m;
• Stage 4
Excavation to level z = −2.40m followed by the installation of the second level of struts
at z = −0.50m;
• Stage 5
Excavation to level z = −5.90m to underside the bottom slab;
• Stage 6
Preparation of the lean concrete level, application of the waterproof membrane and casting
of the foundation slab at level z = −3.78m;
• Stage 7
Removal of the second level of steel struts and casting of lining walls;
• Stage 8
Construction of the intermediate slab. Once the intermediate slab concrete has reached
its design strength, the removal of the ﬁrst level of struts takes place;
Page 146
==PAGE:173==
Chapter 4. Stations
• Stage 9
Construction of the technical level;
• Stage 10
Construction of technical room boxes, emergency stair box and main entrance;
• Stage 11
Completion of the roof slab and backﬁlling.
All secant piles are installed at the same time, including those in both technical rooms.
During excavation, it is ensured that the dewatering is maintained 1m below the working level.

To assess the lateral deformation at each stage, a construction timeline is proposed, based on
the information gathered from engineers at Metroselskabet and that presented by the designer
and listed in Section 4.6.4. The actual start and end dates for each stage are presented in Table

The start date of the installation of the struts corresponds to the day the monitoring software
started recording measurements, whereas the end date corresponds to the date when the load
of the struts stabilized. Moreover, the end date of each stage corresponds to the start of the
subsequent stage. The excavation is an on-going process and the excavation proceeds as the
struts are being installed and pre-loaded. For the purpose of this project, Stages 1 through 8
are considered, focusing on the excavation induced movements.
4.6.5
Monitoring and Instrumentation Overview
Vibenshus Runddel Station is monitored heavily to ensure the stability of the excavation itself,
as well for the eﬀects the excavation and dewatering may have on the settlement of nearby
buildings and the road surfaces.
Page 147
==PAGE:174==
Chapter 4. Stations
For this project, the displacements recorded by the inclinometers and the forces from the
strain gauges are of interest. Inclinometers are used to measure the horizontal displacement
of the secant piles, and the strain gauges record the forces experienced by the struts.
The
monitoring plan layout is presented in Figure 4.6.4.
Figure 4.6.4: Vibenshus Runddel station inclinometer plan.
4.6.5.1
Inclinometers
As shown in Figure 4.6.4, there are eight inclinometers installed in VHR, six are located in the
main box, one in the southern technical room and one in the main entrance. Most inclinome-
ters are installed up to a depth of 39.4m, 3m below the toe of the secant piles, where they are
assumed to be ﬁxed.
For the purpose of the analysis, inclinometers located in the long sides of the main box (21,
22, 25 and 26) are considered. Inclinometer 27, in the southern technical room, is not relevant
because its monitoring data does not match the construction stages of the main box. The short
sides of the wall are not investigated due to the susceptibility to three-dimensional eﬀects, which
may aﬀect the magnitude of the displacements. For the analysis, plane-strain is considered and
this assumption may not be valid for shorter walls (Ou, Chiou, et al., 1996). The following
summarizes the characteristics of each inclinometer:
• VHR-IN21
No data is available for Stage 1. Calibration measurements for the inclinometers start
during Stage 2 at 0.5m intervals up to a depth of 39.5m. From Stage 3 on-wards, recordings
start at 0.4m, followed by 1.4m; recordings are then made every three meters up to a
depth of 39.4m (3.4m below the toe of the wall). Based on its distance to other structures
that may aﬀects the walls behaviour, this inclinometer is the only one considered as a
representation of ‘free-ﬁeld’.
• VHR-IN22
No data is available for Stage 1. Calibration measurements for the inclinometers start
Page 148
==PAGE:175==
Chapter 4. Stations
during Stage 2 at 0.5m intervals up to a depth of 35.5m. From Stage 3 on-wards, recordings
start at 0.4m, followed by 1.4m; recordings are then made every three meters up to a depth
of 34.4m (1.6m above the toe of the wall). From this inclinometer it is not possible to
obtain information regarding the behaviour of the toe of the wall, and the assumption that
it is ﬁxed is not possible. VHR-IN22 is located within the northern technical room; its
proximity to the secant piles of the room might inﬂuence its behaviour.
• VHR-IN25
No data is available for Stage 1. Calibration measurements for the inclinometers start
during Stage 2 at 0.5m intervals up to a depth of 39.5m. From Stage 3 on-wards, recordings
start at 0.4m, followed by 3.4m; recordings are then made every three meters up to a
depth of 39.4m (3.4m below the toe of the wall). VHR-IN25 is located within the southern
technical room; its proximity to the secant piles of the room might inﬂuence its behaviour.
• VHR-IN26
No data is available for Stage 1. Calibration measurements for the inclinometers start
during Stage 2 at 0.5m intervals up to a depth of 39.5m. From Stage 3 on-wards, recordings
start at 0.4m, followed by 3.4m; recordings are then made every three meters up to a
depth of 39.4m (3.4m below the toe of the wall). VHR-IN26 is located within the southern
technical room; its proximity to the secant piles of the room might inﬂuence its behaviour.
Because the calibration data cannot be used, the reliable data starts in Stage 3. Apart fro
this, the inclinometer data proves to be useful and consistent. The location of the inclinometers
in respect to the struts is illustrated in Figure 4.6.5.
Figure 4.6.5: Distance from inclinometers to struts.
4.6.5.2
Strain Gauges
Speciﬁc struts have been selected to be monitored. The designer chose the struts that were
the most representative for the steel works and are the most loaded struts at each level. The
struts are equipped with three strain gauges on each side (six in total). The measured strain is
transformed to stress and load and which are transmitted directly to the Monitoring Database
Kronos. The load cell location is shown in Figure 4.6.6.

4.6.6
Observed Performance
Following the construction timeline in Table 4.6.7, the time elapse showing the cumulative
displacement of the wall with respect to time is obtained. The time elapse for VHR-IN21 is
presented in Figure 4.6.7. Additional ﬁgures are found in Appendix B.
During Stage 3: excavation for the ﬁrst level of struts the largest displacement are recorded
at a distance of 6.4m below the surface, at the ML/MS layer. During the installation of the
ﬁrst level of struts, a displacement inwards is recorded at depths corresponding to the DS2/DG2
layer. The lateral wall movements develop at a constant rate of 0.07mm/day, and do not stabi-
lize until reaching the end of Stage 8: preparation of concrete level.
Similar behaviour may be observed at other locations and summarized by:
• An increase in rate of displacement occurs during Stage 4;
• Maximum displacement generally happens at a depth of 15.4m (DS2/DG2);
• VHR-25 records the maximum displacement at a depth of 12.4 (DS2/DG2).

4.6.6.1
Lateral Wall Movements
As mentioned, VHR-IN21 is consider ‘free-ﬁeld’, Figure 4.6.8 shows the wall’s deformations at
this location throughout construction. Additional ﬁgures can be found in Appendix B.3.1.
All inclinometers show the typical prop-mode deformation, at which the maximum displace-
ment occurs near the excavation (Ou, 2006). Moreover, VHR-IN21 and 26 seem to rotate around
the top slab, where there is a slight change in curvature; this behaviour is not observed in VHR-
IN22 and VHR-IN25. The diﬀerence in behaviour may be attributed to the inﬂuence the secant
piles at the technical room may have in the main box or in the connection between the top slab
and the pile walls.
Page 151
==PAGE:178==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.6.8: Lateral wall movements at VHR-IN21. Dashed red lines represent the excavation depth,
black dashed lines, the struts and thick black line shows the location of the top slab.
The lateral deﬂections of the wall at the end of every stage have been obtained for the four
inclinometers. The deﬂected shape for Stage 8, prior to the casting of the bottom slab can be
seen in Figure 4.6.9, the rest of the stages are found in Appendix B.3.2.
It is clear that the displacements for each inclinometer were fairly similar. The displacements
of the wall vary between 2-10mm and overall the wall deﬂected inwards (towards the excava-
tion). VHR-IN25 recorded the largest displacements during construction. Similar lateral wall
deformation pattern can be observed in the following:
• VHR-IN22 and VHR-IN25;
• VHR-IN21 and VHR-IN26.
The similarities between IN21 and 26 leads to believe that the secant piles in the technical
room close to VHR-IN26 are not inﬂuential.
While the displacements at VHR-IN25 and 26 at shallow depths were smaller than IN21, the
deformations at these inclinometers is larger at deeper depths. It seems as well that the shorter
piles in the technical room close to VHR-IN22 may have increase the stiﬀness of the system,
explaining the smaller deﬂections at this location.
The top slab seems to prevent the lateral movement at the pile head at VHR-IN22 and
VHR-IN25 while rotations are observed at VHR-IN21 and 26. Rotation/restriction occurs along
the same side of the wall, this behaviour may be owed to the connection between the wall and
the slab and the distribution of moment being transfer - the moment transferred close to the
corners will be diﬀerent than the moment transferred at the center of the longitudinal side. At
the locations where the piles were damaged or neglected, the connection was designed as ﬁxed
Page 152
==PAGE:179==
Chapter 4. Stations
with reduce stiﬀness (Frisendahl, 2018) and this would have an impact on the displacement of
the head.
Figure 4.6.9: End of Stage 8: Preparation of Concrete Level. Dashed red lines represent the excavation
depth, black dashed lines, the struts and thick black line shows the location of the top slab.
A summary of the ‘free-ﬁeld’ inclinometer VHR-IN21 is presented in Table 4.6.8. The rela-

stiﬀness of the struts at Vibenshus Runddel with the short unsupported length of the retaining

Bending Moment Distribution
The bending moment of the secant wall is approximated based on the polynomial function
describing the deﬂected shape of the wall shown in Figure 4.6.10.
Figure 4.6.10: Sixth-order polynomial function for the end of Stage 8.
The Ftool model is shown in Figure 4.6.11, the loads are as presented by the designer: the
self-weight of the structure 16kPa and the variable loads of 15kPa. The connection between the
top slab and the wall was designed as ﬁxed with reduced stiﬀness. If the piles were damaged or
neglected, the connection was pinned at the top. For the ﬁxed connection, there is a moment
induced by the load situated at the top slab. This moment is applied at the top of the wall as
explained in Section 3.2.

Figure 4.6.12 shows the moment distribution for the both scenarios described above. For
the ﬁxed connection, the maximum moment occurs at the top of the pile, due to the induced
moment of the top slab, however the maximum negative and minimum values withing the wall
are of ≈-460kNm/m at z=-34.7m and ≈333kNm/m at z=-20.2m, respectively. While for the
pinned connections, the maximum negative and positive bending moment are ≈-270kNm/m
at z=-34.0m and ≈204kNm/m at z=-18.3m, respectively.
While this approximation has its
limitations and the moment distribution may not be exact, the values are well below the design
values which also correlates with the smaller actual displacements.

4.6.6.4
Strut Performance
The central struts are considered to have the same target loads, hence, Load Cell C is investigated
and assumed to describe the behaviour of the other central struts, and loads can be investigated
with respect to the displacements at VHR-IN21 and 26. The load history is presented in Figure
4.6.13. The gradual increase in force on both struts prior to reaching their peak load matches
to that increase in displacement during Stage 3 through 7.
The peak load at the ﬁrst and
second level of struts, 2550kN and 1050kN respectively, coincides with the time the wall reaches
its maximum displacement as it stabilized during Stage 8. This behaviour is observed in all
four inclinometers (IN21, 22, 25 and 26). VHR-IN21 and 22 are located in proximity to the
Page 155
==PAGE:182==
Chapter 4. Stations
struts. These inclinometers presented similar deﬂected shapes and although IN22 is located at
the edge of the main excavation, it presents similar behaviour to VHR-IN21 and ‘jumps´ in the
displacement follows the increase in load shown in Figure 4.6.13.
Figure 4.6.13: Load Cell C: strut load changes during construction.
4.6.7
Discussion of Performance
The ﬁnal construction stages, after casting the slabs, removing struts and breaking of the tracks
have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the ﬁnal lateral maximum displacement. Figure 4.6.14 depicts the
maximum lateral deformation relative to the maximum lateral deformation at completion. The
maximum lateral movement occurs during Stage 14 : Break in Track 1 at a depth of 9.4m at the
middle meltwater sand/gravel unit (DS2/DG3). The lateral movement at the end of excavation
(Stage 8) accounts for 58% of the maximum lateral movement of the secant wall. The abrupt
change in displacement corresponds to the removal of the second level of struts.

Figure 4.6.14: Lateral deformation behaviour relative to construction progress.
The observed performance in terms of maximum lateral wall movements as the excavation
progresses is shown in Figure 4.6.15. The upper and lower bounds correspond to VHR-IN25
and VHR-IN22, respectively.
The results are discussed in relation to empirical correlations
mentioned in Chapter 2. The horizontal moment to excavation depth ratio (δh/He) reported
by Clough and O’Rourke (1990) ranged from 0.2 to 0.3% for excavations in stiﬀsoils using stiﬀ
support systems such as diaphragm walls. Vibenshus Runddel presents δh/He ratios between
0.037% to 0.055% if only excavation activity is considered. However, the stratigraphy considered
by Clough and O’Rourke (1990) does not describe the transitional soils found in VHR.
Figure 4.6.15: Observed maximum lateral wall deformations with respect to excavation depth.
The performance is also analyzed in terms of the relative and eﬀective stiﬀness for Stages
2 through 8, as deﬁned in Section 3.3. The system stiﬀness with respect to normalized lateral
deﬂection is presented in Figures 4.6.16 and 4.6.17. During the typical construction, the defor-
Page 157
==PAGE:184==
Chapter 4. Stations
mation of the wall was mostly inﬂuenced by the stiﬀness of the wall. The system stiﬀness is
deﬁned by the following:
• EI: bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall as per design:
EI=204MPa;
• Es: stiﬀness of the soil layer at which the maximum deﬂection occur - Eur from Table
4.4.4;
• Eseq: equivalent soil stiﬀness as per deﬁned in Chapter 3, Section 3.3.
Eseq= 505MPa;
• havg: weighted average spacing between supports at any given stage.
The system stiﬀness and the lateral wall movements are studied for VHR-IN21, 22, 25 and
26. The upper bound of the data set corresponds to VHR-IN25, the lower bound corresponds
to VHR-IN22. There is a positive relationship between the system stiﬀness to the displacement.
Due to the small retained height, havg, the initial stages have low system stiﬀness and smaller
defections.
During the ﬁrst excavation the relative stiﬀness is low due to the large unsupported excava-
tion depth. During the installation of the struts (Stages 4 and 6) the stiﬀness increases due to
a decrease in spacing of the struts and an increase in the soil layer stiﬀness. In the cases where
the maximum displacements occurred in the same soil layer, the system stiﬀness only depends
on the spacing between the supports which increases during excavation and decreases during
installation of struts. However, the variation between normalized displacements is minimal.
Figure 4.6.16: Relative stiﬀness compared to normalized displacement.
Page 158
==PAGE:185==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.6.17: Relative equivalent stiﬀness compared to normalized displacement.
4.6.8
Summary and Conclusions
Vibenshus Runddel is a relatively shallow station with an excavation of 18.8m supported by
two levels of struts and 36m long secant pile walls. The toe of the wall extends to cut oﬀhigh
ﬂow zones (below the UCL I layer). The site is dominated by layers of meltwater sand/gravel
and the occurrence of interstadial clay units, these layers are relatively stiﬀ. The results of the
investigation is summarized as follows:
• The UCL I layer is encountered a depth of z=-30m;
• δhmax varies between 7-0-9.2mm at the end of the excavation stages;
• δhmax occur withing the DS/DG layer at 0.7Hex;
• δhmax/Hex varies between 0.037-0.055%.
While VHR-IN21 is considered a ‘free-ﬁeld’ inclinometer, the recorded displacements at this
location are among the lowest, this could mean that its proximity to the secant walls of the
northern technical room lead to arching eﬀects that reduced the displacements of the wall.
In the contrary, VHR-IN25 and 26 which are located in the main box next to the southern
technical room presented the largest deﬂections. The distance between the technical room and
the main box walls may be enough to prevent any interaction, allowing the deformations to
develop without external inﬂuence.
Page 159
==PAGE:186==
Chapter 4. Stations
4.7
Østerport
Østerport metro station (KK) is located next to Oslo Plads, between Kristianiagade and Østbane-
gade, as seen in Figure 4.7.1. The main staircase is along Kristianiagade and has connection to
the existing train station to the southeast via an underground transfer tunnel. The residential
building to the East of the station is at a distance of < 10m and it is relevant to the retaining
wall design calculations. Østerport station is also the starting point for the Nordhavn Metro
extension.
Figure 4.7.1: Østerport Station site shown within the blue lines (taken from Google Earth)
4.7.1
Design Features
Østerport is a wide underground station with a width of 20.6m between lining walls and a length
of 68.1m. As other stations in the Cityringen, the construction is carried out with a permanent
top slab and temporary struts to prevent excessive settlements of the nearby buildings. The
main features of the station are summarized as follows (all levels are in m DVR90):
• Ground Level is at z≈+7.3m;
• Area of the shaft is approximately 1404m2;
• Reinforced secant pile walls with a cut oﬀdistance of 27m (embedment depth: 2.4m);
• Excavation level within the station at z≈-17.5m (He = 24.6m).
4.7.2
Geotechnical Conditions
The identiﬁcation of the individual layers has been based on the geological description in com-
bination with the classiﬁcation tests and the results of the geophysical tests carried out in situ
in the boreholes identiﬁed in Figure 4.7.2. The soil types listed in Table 4.7.1 are encountered
in the site and Figure 4.7.3 illustrates the ground conditions. A detailed geological description
is presented in Appendix G.1. The main geotechnical characteristics of the site are summarized
below:
• The ﬁll thickness (FY) varies from 1.7-6m with thickness up to 10.5m in the northeast;
• The ﬁll is underlain by a 0.3-4.5m thick layer of upper clay till with sand till and sand
(ML1);
• The ML1 layer is followed by a 2.6-9m thick layers of meltwater sand and gravel (DS2/DG2);
• Transition from UCL to MCL is located at approximately z=-20m;
Page 160
==PAGE:187==
Chapter 4. Stations
• Till deposits may contain cobbles and boulders.
Figure 4.7.2: Boreholes information plan at Østerport. Green double line marks location of the project
area and thick purple lines mark the approximate boundaries between ravelin, moat and foreland.
Table 4.7.1: Soil and limestone encountered at Østerport (Metroselskabet, 2014c).
Page 161
==PAGE:188==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.7.3: Layering of soil and limestone at Østerport parallel to the tunnel axis. The DL1, DL2
and ML2 units are not shown due to the limited thickness and extent (Metroselskabet, 2014c).
The project area is close to the present Østerport train station where part of the former
defensive system of Copenhagen was situated. The system consisted of zigzag running ramparts
with a water ﬁlled moat in front, as seen in Figure 4.7.4 (Metroselskabet, 2014c). According to
‘DGF Bulleting 19, p116’ (Frederiksen et al., 2002), the moat was a single trench with water
level at approx. z=+0.6m and bottom between levels z=-1.9 and z=-2.8m. At water level, the
moat was 38m wide, this has been assessed in the design proﬁles by the designer. The project
area can also be seen in Figure 4.7.2. A detail geological description is found in Appendix G.1.

foreland compared to the present streets. (Metroselskabet, 2014c).
4.7.2.1
Design Proﬁles
Several design proﬁles have been established in the ‘Østerport - Geotechnical Design Report’
based on the stratigraphy revealed by the borings on site. These proﬁles are shown in Table
4.7.2.
Table 4.7.2: Design Proﬁles for Østerport (Metroselskabet, 2014c).
The designer deemed Proﬁles 1 and 2 the most critical for the assessment of stress and
deformation for retaining walls. The designer has selected the proﬁles based on the greater
thickness of overconsolidated layers and in particular the ML layer, which maximizes thrust on
the retaining wall. Moreover, the ML layer is located within the secondary aquifer at which the
hydraulic pressure is greater than at other proﬁles.
The characteristic soil properties for the design of the retaining wall is presented in Table 4.7.3
and a justiﬁcation on the assessment is found in Appendix G.2. The parameters are based on the

have been performed in order to evaluate the inﬂuence of the most important geotechnical

selected values. According to the GIR Level III, the geological parameters above vary between
the values reported in Table 4.7.4.

4.7.2.2
Water Levels
Following the speciﬁcations in GIR Level I and III, the groundwater levels shown in Table 4.7.5
should be applied for the retaining wall design. In periods of high precipitation, the piezometric
head in the secondary aquifer can be at or even above the ground level. The primary water
table is considered for the SLS and ULS analyses because the secondary water table is in close
proximity. For the ALS analysis, the piezometric head in the primary and secondary aquifer is
considered to ensure a safe working place and the stability of structures. The retaining structures
are designed to withstand the full hydrostatic water pressure acting behind the walls.
Page 164
==PAGE:191==
Chapter 4. Stations
Table 4.7.5: Design groundwater tables (Metroselskabet, 2014c).
4.7.3
Retaining System Characteristics
Østerport permanent structure includes a continuous wall constructed by means of secant piles,
properly overlapped up to a depth of 27m and a series of permanent slabs, cast after completion.
Retaining Wall System
The continuous secant piles are constructed with secant primary and secondary piles. The main
station has 1m diameter secant piles at 785 and 800mm horizontal spacing.
• Length: 27m;
• Diameter: 1m;
• Horizontal spacing: 785/800mm (longitudinal/transversal wall);
• Moment of Inertia (785mm): 0.062531m4/m;
• Moment of Inertia (800mm): 0.061359m4/m;
• Young´s Modulus (primary and secondary piles): 34GPa (C35/45).
Permanent Slabs
The retaining wall is supported in its permanent state by the interior structures, which includes
slabs at diﬀerent levels of the station. The thickness of the slabs are summarized below.
• Roof Level: Varies with an equivalent area of Aeq = 0.87m2/m;
• Concourse Level: 0.60m;
• Technical Level: 0.40m;
• Intermediate Level: 1.8m;
• Platform Level: 0.25m;
• Below Platform Level: Varies.
In the permanent stage, only the roof slab will be connected to the retaining walls and is
allowed to act in tension/compression.
4.7.3.1
Temporary Support Systems
Similar to NOR and AMH, three level of struts are installed from axis C to J (Figure 4.7.5) while
four level of struts are placed at the left and right of the top slab. The struts are designed to
prevent any excessive settlement during construction and are kept in compression by means of
hydraulic jacks during construction. The steel tubes used during construction have the following
characteristics:

• Spacing

Main Entrance - 4.5m
All struts have a Young’s Modulus of Es = 210MPa. After installation, a prestressing force
of 550kN is applied to each strut.
Figure 4.7.5: Plan view of the steel strut - Østerport Station (Metroselskabet, 2014a)

4.7.5
Monitoring and Instrumentation Overview
The investigation and interpretation of data focuses on the observations of horizontal deforma-
tions of the secant wall measured by inclinometers and in the monitoring data for the temporary
bracing system from the strain gauges (load cells), which reveal information on the forces acting
in the struts. A plan view showing the location of the inclinometers is presented in Figure 4.7.6.
Figure 4.7.6: Østerport station: inclinometer plan.
4.7.5.1
Inclinometers
A total of eight inclinometers are used to monitor the station, shown in Figure 4.7.6. Typically,
the inclinometers are placed within the secant pile and extend up to a depth of 31.4m and are
considered ﬁxed at this point.
Three inclinometers are located within the longitudinal walls and one on each of the transver-
sal walls of the station. Only the inclinometers on the long side of the main box are interpreted,
assuming two-dimensional plane strain behaviour (21, 22, 23 ; 25, 26 and 27). The deforma-
tion behaviour at the central section of the shorter sides, aﬀected by concrete arching eﬀect,
is three-dimensional and the displacement should be much smaller (Ou, 2006). Moreover, the
inclinometers in the western side of the station are considered ‘free-ﬁeld’ because there are no
existing structures in their vicinity that may inﬂuence their behaviour.
The measurement frequency is set to 4 hours.
For the purpose of this analysis, 2 daily
measurements were considered. The manual recordings for all inclinometers occur at 0.4m, 1.4m
and then with 3m intervals. The following summarizes the characteristics of the inclinometers:
• INC 21
No data for Stage 1. Located within the top slab. Inclinometer extents up to z=-31.4m.
• INC 22
No data for Stage 1. Located within the top slab. Inclinometer extents up to z=-28.4m.
Page 168
==PAGE:195==
Chapter 4. Stations
• INC 23
No data is available for Stages 1. Located between the J and K axis with no support at
the pile head. Inclinometer extents up to z=-25.4m (within the wall).
• INC 25
No data is available for Stages 1. Located between the J and K axis with no support
at the pile head. The inclinometer is considered ‘free-ﬁeld’. Inclinometer extents up to
z=-31.4m.
• INC 26
No data for is available Stage 1. Located within the top slab and the inclinometer extents
up to z=-28.4m. The inclinometer is considered ‘free-ﬁeld’.
• INC 27
No data for is available Stage 1. Located within the top slab. The inclinometer is con-
sidered ‘free-ﬁeld’.
After the end of Stage 10: Preparation of concrete level, a month
of measurements is missing (June 13,2015- July 23, 2015). Inclinometer extents up to
z=-31.4m
Apart for the lack of data for several stages, the inclinometers measurements show reliable
for all other stages. The inclinometer distance from the temporary bracing system is shown in
Figure 4.7.7.
Figure 4.7.7: Distance from inclinometers to struts.
4.7.5.2
Strain Gauges
The strut loads are measured with three sets of strain gauges on each side of the selected struts
chosen by the CMT designers which are critical and experience the highest loads in the design.
The struts have the same characteristics and similar level of action is expected in the central
part of the station. The load cells are located at the struts in axis C,D, J and K. Moreover, all
the diagonal struts are monitored to understand the corner behaviour. Figure 4.7.8 shows the
location of the monitored struts.
Page 169
==PAGE:196==
Chapter 4. Stations
Figure 4.7.8: Location of strain gauges on struts (Metroselskabet, 2014e).
The designer noted that the 1st level of temporary bracing is not monitored. As anticipated
in other stations (AMH and NOR), the monitoring activities should focus mainly in the lower
struts and not to the ones connected at top slab elevation. This is based on the following:
• The struts at roof level are generally exposed to direct solar radiation or variable air tem-
perature, which leads to uncertain measurements, sudden load ﬂuctuations and potentially
repeated alarms.
• These struts are over-dimensioned with respect to the design compressive loads to account
for the potential load increments due to natural thermal variations.
4.7.6
Observed Performance
The cumulative displacement at KK-IN25 and KK-IN26 are presented in Figure 4.7.9 and 4.7.10.
The monitoring data has been processed and it revealed that the displacements were overall
larger at KK-IN25. This inclinometer is also considered ‘free-ﬁeld’ and is good representation
of the behaviour of the other inclinometers. Additional ﬁgures can be found in Appendix G.3.
KK-IN25 revealed that at shallow excavations and early stages, most of the movements oc-
curred above 10m and are small displacements (δh < 2.0mm). The maximum displacement
during Stage 2: top slab casted through Stage 7: excavation for third level of struts, occur at a
depth of 7.4m (ML/MS). A slight increase in displacement is observed during mid-Stage 7, which
is attributed to an increase of unsupported excavation. During this time, the largest displace-
ment is recorded at a depth of 16.4m within the UCL (HP or I) layer. From Stage 3: excavation
for ﬁrst level of struts
through 7, there is a rate of displacement less than 0.01mm/day after
which there is a time of stability between the end of Stage 7 and the Stage 8: installation of
third level of struts. This rate later increases to 0.8mm/day until stabilizing during Stage 10.
During Stage 10, there is an increase of (∆δh = 3mm) at a depth ranging from 10.4-16.4m and
seem to be a localized event.
The largest displacements at KK-IN26 are always recorded at deeper depths. From Stages
3 through 8, δmax is recorded between 10.4m and 13.4m. The rate of displacement during Stage
7, at which the most relevant increase occur, is around 0.10mm/day. Jumps occur during ex-
cavation Stages 7 and 9 while during installation of bracing, the displacement stabilizes. The
sudden increase in displacement during excavation may be owed to the pre-loading of the struts
that takes place as excavation proceeds. Excavation generally leads to smooth change in dis-
placements and the sudden lateral movement is usually attributed to an increase in lateral load
introduced by the struts.
Similar behaviour is observed in all other inclinometers with except for KK-IN22.
This
inclinometer showed large inward displacements at the head of the wall.

4.7.6.1
Lateral Wall Movements
The ‘free-ﬁeld’ inclinometers considered in this study are KK-IN25 and KK-IN26. KK-IN26
is located within the top slab, allowing a comparison between the other stations, whereas the
wall at KK-IN25 is unsupported at the pile head. The lateral wall movements recorded during
Page 171
==PAGE:198==
Chapter 4. Stations
construction are presented in Figures 4.7.11 and 4.7.12. Additional lateral deformation proﬁles
are presented in Appendix G.3.1.
KK-25 remains ‘ﬁxed’ at the head of the pile up to Stage 4 and having a prop-mode de-
formation; because the wall is not supported at the top by a slab, it is a rare occurrence the
fact that there is no cantilevered-like behaviour during early excavation stages but the author
acknowledges that the early data is missing and no conclusion can be drawn from this. After the
excavation reached a depth of 10.73m, the wall experiences a deep inward movement at shallow
depths and δmax shifts to deeper depths. The wall seems to always be ﬁxed below the excavation
depths.
In contrast, at KK-IN27, the top of the wall has the opposite behaviour, rotating at the
head and having an inward movement which can be attributed to the ﬁx connection of the top
slab and the wall. A rotation occurs at around 4m and as the wall is ‘ﬁxed’ below z=10.73m.
The point of rotation remains at z=4m but as excavation progresses, the wall is ﬁxed at deeper
depths and the maximum lateral displacements also shift to deeper depths. This behaviour is
in part owed to the softer soils at shallow ground that underlain very stiﬀsoils and rock.
During the installation of the third level of struts (Stage 7) the change in lateral movement
occurred at shallow depths, where KK-IN22 turned inwards while the rest of the inclinometers
moved towards the retained soil. This change occurs above the location of the ﬁrst level of
struts. If the stiﬀness of the strut is high enough, the compression of the struts may be small,
and the retaining wall will rotate about this point and this may lead to this type of deformation,
however it does not explain the diﬀerence in behaviour shown by KK-IN22.

4.7.6.3
Bending Moment Distribution
The station was initially designed as pinned, however no other information regarding the connec-
tion (Frisendahl, 2018). The Ftool model is presented in Figure 4.7.14, the uniform distributed
load is as speciﬁed in the Metroselskabet (2014a).
Figure 4.7.14: Ftool model for Østerport with a maximum moment at the ﬁxed ends of 998.2kNm.
The lateral wall deformation is presented in Figure 4.2.16: the polynomial slightly over-
estimates the displacements below z=-19.4m by less than 2mm which does not have a signiﬁcant
inﬂuence to the approximation of the bending moment. Following the methodology presented
in Section 3.2 the moment distributions for the pinned and ﬁxed conditions are computed and
presented in Figure 4.7.16. A ‘ﬁxed’ connection is never truly fully ﬁxed, and there will be
some moment that is to be carried by the slab and part of it to be transferred to the pile and
redistributed in the pile cap. The behaviour of the wall is then somewhere in between a pinned
and a fully ﬁxed connections presented below.
For a pinned connection, the maximum negative and positive bending moment are ≈-
207kNm/m at z=-4.0m and ≈278kNm/m at z=-16.3m, respectively; the fully ﬁxed connec-
tion yields maximum bending moments within the wall of ≈-225kNm/m at z=-28.0m and
≈324kNm/m at z=-17.1m .

4.7.7
Discussion of Performance
The following analyses seek to identify major trends in the monitoring data. The investigation
focuses in the excavation induced movements, but it is known that activity after completion will
have an impact on the structure. Figure 4.7.19 presents the maximum lateral movement relative
to the maximum lateral deformation at the end of the project. The maximum lateral movement
(δhmax = 18.1mm) occurs during Stage 21: Concourse slab casted at a depth of 16.4m. The
lateral movement at the end of Stage 10 accounts for the 34% of the maximum movement. In
this station, the majority of the displacements occur after the excavation stages which is outside
the scope of this investigation, but it is important to reinforce the idea that are several factors
that will impact the magnitude of displacement of a deep excavation and that this investigation
focuses in limited factors.

Figure 4.7.20 presents the normalized lateral wall movement versus excavation depth for
the ‘free-ﬁeld’ inclinometers. The relationships found here are among the lowest the Cityringen
station interpreted for this report. An evident trend is observed in the data. Similar relationships
of δhmax/He ratio reported by Clough and O’Rourke (1990) ranged between 0.2% and 0.3%. The
relationship at Østerport station is signiﬁcantly less than that presented. Long (2001) found that
for prop walls, the average δhmax were 0.17%He, whereas Moormann (2004) ﬁndings are closer
to that of Clough and O’Rourke (1990), the average value is δhmax/He = 0.27%. The relations
mentioned are based on a wide range of soil conditions, excavation support and bracing systems,
and following diﬀerent construction sequence which should be considered only as reference values.

Further analysis leads to the relationship of the system stiﬀness to the lateral wall movements,
Page 180
==PAGE:207==
Chapter 4. Stations
a per Section 3.3, shown in Figure 4.7.21 and 4.7.22. The system stiﬀness is deﬁned by the
following:
• EI: bending stiﬀness of the retaining wall as per design:
EI=2,064MPa;
• Es: stiﬀness of the soil layer at which the maximum deﬂection occur - Eur from Table
4.7.3;
• Eseq: equivalent soil stiﬀness as per deﬁned in Section 3.3 in Chapter 3:
Eseq= 853MPa;
• havg: weighted average spacing between supports at any given stage.
The lower values correspond to KK-IN26 and the higher to KK-IN25. The height system
stiﬀness are attributed to the shallow excavations (small havg) and having the maximum dis-
placement located at shallow depths. Similar to Nuuks Plads, there is a large scatter in the
data, and it seems that movement is independent of the system stiﬀness. A possible explanation
for the lack of dependency on system stiﬀness: once suﬃcient stiﬀness is available, movement
is determined by the magnitude of excavation base heave. Also, the UCL layer is encountered
at a relatively shallow depth (≃15m) and this stiﬀsoil has signiﬁcant capacity to self-support
(Long, 2001). A tendency is observed for the data to scattered around 0.001 < ρeq < 0.01,
regardless of the stage (i.e strut spacing, number of excavation depth), again the displacements
do not seem to depend on these deﬁnitions of system stiﬀness.

4.7.8
Summary and Conclusions
Østerport is deep excavation has a depth of 24.6m and supported by walls up to 27m long. The
top slab is casted in the central part of the excavation and struts are positioned at the edges.
The station is then supported by 4 levels of struts at the exterior and 3 levels within the interior
of the main excavation, below the slab. The stratigraphy of the site is dominated by the middle
meltwater sand/gravel which inﬂuences the relative stiﬀness of the system. The main results
are summarized below:
• The Upper Copenhagen Limestone is encountered at a depth of z=-15.3m;
• The overall maximum displacement δhmax at the end of excavations ranged between 4.7-
6.4mm;
• The δhmax/Hex ratio varies between 0.01-0.025Hex%;
• δhmax was located within the UCL I layer, at approximately 0.6Hex.
All inclinometers showed similar behaviour except for KK-IN22. At all other locations, the
wall is ﬁxed at the top slab and the walls show deep inward movement while for KK-IN22 there
seems to be no inﬂuence from the top slab - this may be due to the connection between the pile
head and the slab. The inclinometer is also located along the residential building side which has
further inﬂuence on the behaviour of the wall.
Due to the occurrence of stiﬀmaterial, the relative stiﬀness of the system is quite high and
this leads to smaller displacements. Moreover, the change in displacement coincides with the
behaviour of the struts, the loads stabilize as the displacements do.
There is also large scattering in the relative stiﬀness and normalized displacement plots,
seemingly no relationship between the system stiﬀness and the displacement.
According to
Long (2001) an explanation of the lack of dependency on system stiﬀness is that, once suﬃcient
stiﬀness is available, movement is governed by base heave and that stiﬀsoils have the ability to
‘arch’ or ‘self-support’.
Page 182
==PAGE:209==
Chapter 5
Comparison of Stations
This chapter focuses on the comparison of the relevant results from the stations presented in
Chapter 4.
The comparison and interpretation leads to present a relationship between the
maximum displacement, wall and soil stiﬀness and excavation height.
5.1
Literature Review
It was concluded in the literature review that the current practice to predict lateral deforma-
tion based on empirical relationships could lead to conservative or non-representative results.
The ﬁeld observations have been compared to Clough and O’Rourke (1990) and Ou, Hsieh,
et al. (1993) as well for Long (2001) and Moormann (2004) databases. Figure 5.1.1 and 5.1.2
show the ﬁeld observations at the end of the excavation stages (Preparation of concrete levels)
plotted against the system stiﬀness deﬁned by Clough and O’Rourke (1990).
The normalized lateral wall deformations as a function of the system stiﬀness proposed by
Clough and O’Rourke (1990) show that the excavations have a factor of safety well above 3.
Nørrebros Runddel, due to the large displacements presented by the wall, are within the F.S of
3. While Nørrebros Runddel again plots above the line for FOS of 3 in Figure 5.1.2, the stations
with stiﬀer stratigraphies are all well below this limit.
183
==PAGE:210==
Chapter 5. Comparison of Stations
Figure 5.1.1: Field observations plotted against Clough and O’Rourke (1990) relationship between the
maximum deﬂections of walls, stiﬀness of strutting systems and factor of safety against basal heave.
Figure 5.1.2: Field observations with the case histories from Long (2001).
Similarly, Figures 5.1.3, 5.1.4 and 5.1.5 present the maximum displacement with respect to
excavation depth for the last stage of excavation while all ﬁeld observation are plotted in Figure
5.1.6.
In discussions in Chapter 4, it was clear that δh/He for all stations was smaller than that
Page 184
==PAGE:211==
Chapter 5. Comparison of Stations
predicted by literature. The displacements presented by the secant pile walls in the Cityringen
are signiﬁcantly smaller than the case histories that have been studied. Evidently, the empirical
relationships of current practice do not capture the behaviour of secant pile walls in a mixed
soil stratigraphy such as Copenhagen’s subsoil.
Figure 5.1.7 show that δh/He for the seven stations studied range between 0.01-0.12%He,
while six of them present very similar displacements (Figure 5.1.8). This range is in agreement
with the ﬁndings by Carder (1995) where for excavations in stiﬀclay using contiguous piling,
ranged between 0.04-0.16%He. It is possible that the stiﬀness characteristics of the case stud-
ies are similar to those treated in this thesis. The normalized displacements with respect to
excavation depth are illustrated in Figure 5.1.8. The values are well below those proposed by
Long (2001).

5.2
System Stiﬀness
The scope of Chapter 4 was to identify major trends in the data, taking into account the mea-
surements that were representative of the project. In an eﬀort to describe the data, a system
stiﬀness factor, ρ, was proposed in Section 3.3. The data for all stations was then analyzed in
these terms. The system stiﬀness to normalized displacement for all stations at the ﬁnal exca-
vation depth is presented in Figures 5.2.1 and 5.2.2. Additional ﬁgures showing relative stiﬀness
per stage for all stations can be found in Appendix H.2.
A trend is observed having Nørrebros Runddel as the upper limit. The high values of relative
stiﬀness at NOR are attributed to the relatively soft soils encountered at the site, which reduced
the equivalent system stiﬀness signiﬁcantly when compared to the other stations.
When comparing these plots and the ones presented in Appendix H.2 it can be observed
that at certain stages, several stations present similar relative stiﬀness, either ρ∗or ρeq, but
the δh/He ratio varies. This event becomes more apparent after Stage 4 possibly due to the
increase in strength of the top slab which provides stability to the system and displacements
in most stations increase steadily. Typically, the stations that yield similar system stiﬀness are
Frederiksberg (FB), Aksel Møllers Have and Nuuks Plads (NUP) and at times Østerport seem
to follow similar behaviour.
Among the factors that may aﬀect the magnitude of δhmax between the stations are:
• Ground water table conditions;
• Subsoil conditions;
Page 188
==PAGE:215==
Chapter 5. Comparison of Stations
• Excavation geometry;
• Excavation sequence;
• Surcharge conditions (i.e existence of adjacent buildings);
• Wall penetration depth;
• Stiﬀness of retaining wall;
• Type of lateral support (i.e spacing, stiﬀness, time to pre-stress).
Considering only the ‘free-ﬁeld’ inclinometers, it is safe to assume that the surcharge condi-
tions for all stations are fairly similar. However, nearby structures such as residential buildings
may have an impact on the behaviour of the excavation and this should be acknowledge.
The excavations’ geometry of the stations in Cityringen are similar. NUP and AMH have
the exact dimensions and Østerport is the widest station at 20.6m followed by FB at 18.5m.
While Østerport is the widest station, its displacements are among the smallest when compared
to the others leading to the conclusion that this may not be a greatly inﬂuential factor.
The increase in wall stiﬀness helps to reduce the lateral wall deﬂections.
Østerport has
among the highest bending stiﬀness (as for NOR), followed by FB, AMH and NUP. The lateral
support system through Cityringen is similar as is the method of construction and installation
(i.e unsupported length of excavation for supports, pre-load, dimensions and spacing). More-
over, the excavation depth of these stations is similar.
The main focus is then the diﬀerence in stratigraphy among the sites. Figures summarizing
the subsoil and excavation characteristics for the stations are presented in Appendix H.1. Having
considered the factors above, the following is concluded:
• Nearby buildings have a signiﬁcant impact on the deep excavations, specially at FB
(Hariri, 2016);
• The presence of stiﬀmaterial such as UCL in the excavation depth heavily inﬂuence the
displacements;
• Short lengths of unsupported excavation leads to smaller displacements;
• Thick layers of soft material at shallow depths allows for deep outward movement at the
top of the wall.
Page 189
==PAGE:216==
Chapter 5. Comparison of Stations
Figure 5.2.1: End of Excavation: Relative Stiﬀness compared to normalized displacements.
Figure 5.2.2: End of Excavation: Relative Eﬀective Stiﬀness compared to normalized displacements.
The same trend-line proposed on the ﬁgures above is then plotted against all other stages
of the excavation process, in Figures 5.2.3 and 5.2.4. In general, the observations follow the
predicted trend curves, although there is some indication that the curve may be conservative
when using the ρ∗and under-predict displacements in soft ground when using the ρeq. Because
the excavation was an ongoing process, there is a level of uncertainty with attributed to He.
Furthermore, it has been mentioned that at shallow excavation depths, the system stiﬀness was
high and did not follow any particular trend. An eﬀort was made to establish a relationship
between the data with this type of behaviour, and an additional regression line has been ﬁtted
to the low deﬂection/high system stiﬀness situations.

The relative equivalent stiﬀness is re-deﬁned as per Section 3.3.3 to try to ﬁnd a better relation-

The regression line proposed before has been plotted against the re-deﬁned relative eﬀective
stiﬀness and the data appears to ﬁt here better than with the other relationships - the scatters
around the regression line which presents an average approximation of the displacements.
With the analysis presented above and the interpretation of the stations in Chapter 4 similar
conclusion than to those above are drawn:
• All stations present similar EI and axial stiﬀness of the struts, EA;
• Smaller deﬂections are recorded when the equivalent system stiﬀness is high (stiﬀer soils);
• The location of the limestone seems to aﬀect the magnitude of the displacements;
• Soft shallow ground allows the outward movement of the wall.
5.3
Lateral Wall Deformation
The lateral wall displacements for the chosen inclinometers are presented in Figure 5.3.1. For
most stations, the maximum displacements occur at half-way the wall length, except at Viben-
shus Runddel. VHR is a relatively shallow excavation but due to the required cut-oﬀlength to
prevent basal failure, the wall length is signiﬁcantly longer than in the other stations, and this
may have contributed to the characteristics of its deformed shape. The lateral wall displace-
ments are normalized with respect to the maximum lateral displacement recorded at the end of
the preparation of the concrete level while the depth is normalized with respect to:
• The ﬁnal excavation depth, He, in Figure 5.3.2;
• The wall length, Hw, in Figure 5.3.3.
A common occurrence in all stations is that the maximum displacement is located at 0.6-
0.7Hex, agreeing with Carder (1995) ﬁndings. It is possible to obtain the layer at which the
Page 192
==PAGE:219==
Chapter 5. Comparison of Stations
maximum displacement will occur using Figure 5.3.3 and subsequently use the relative stiﬀness
charts presented in Section 5.2 to approximate the maximum displacement.

‘free-ﬁeld’ inclinometers.

Figure 5.3.3: Normalized displacement with respect

5.4
Limitations
The data presented above was interpreted following an observational method.
Some of the
limitations to the approach are:
• Stiﬀness of struts or level of pre-loading are not considered;
• The subsoil conditions is based on the stratigraphy chosen by the designer, this might not
describe the local soil proﬁle at each inclinometer;
• Three-dimensional eﬀects are disregarded;
• The deﬁnition of the construction stages is at times subjective and the excavation/instal-
lation of struts typically overlap, this means that the maximum displacements may not be
attributed to one event, rather a combination of both;
• Installation errors: some inclinometers were not ﬁxed at the ends;
• The inﬂuence of the lowering of the water table within the excavation was not investigated;
• The method is solely observational and the soil-structure interaction is not investigated.
In summary, deep excavations are inﬂuenced by a large number of factors that inﬂuence the
behavior of the excavation, which makes the design a multi-dimensional task. An excavation is
also inﬂuenced by the workmanship or deviations that might have not been mentioned in the
design reports. All of these factors contribute to the diﬃcult task of properly quantifying ground
deformations empirically. At times, the investigation failed to isolate many major variables such
as the pre-load eﬀects or to investigate soil-structure interaction mechanisms involved in the
performance of deep excavation construction.
Page 194
==PAGE:221==
Chapter 6
Numerical Model
The total deformation of a retaining wall is a complex soil-structure interaction problem. The
quantitative analysis of ground movements associated with excavation support is diﬃcult and
recent advancement in numerical analysis have allowed designers to modify certain parameters
while keeping others constant, allowing to overcome the limitations of the observational method
(Zapata-Medina, 2007). The focus of this chapter is to investigate the measured displacements
with the pseudo-ﬁnite element software Frew in order to back-analyze the stiﬀness of the soil
layers.
Chapter 4 showed that the measured displacements were well below the design estimates and
computer modeling is used to investigate the design parameters used. The modeling of three
stations is carried out in an eﬀort to predict the measured displacements by modifying certain
parameters.
Among the factors that may aﬀect the behaviour of the wall compared to that estimated by
the design are:
• Stratigraphy;
• Strength of geotechnical parameters;
• Stiﬀness of geotechnical parameters;
• Retaining wall bending stiﬀness;
• Groundwater conditions;
• Temporary/permanent support system.
The analysis focuses mainly in the stiﬀness of the soil layers and more speciﬁcally, in the
limestone (UCL). It has been noted by the designer that the stiﬀness parameters for all stations
are conservative. The stations chosen for the analysis are:
• Aksel Møllers Have - UCL encountered at z=-14.75m
• Østerport - UCL encountered at z=-15.3m
• Vibenshus Runddel - UCL encountered at z=-30m.
The stations were mainly chosen based on the location of the limestone and the displacements
measured at this layer. Only the inclinometers considered ‘free-ﬁeld’ are investigated and the
side of the wall on which they are located is modelled. For this reason, only the backﬁll load is
included for the analysis. The analysis is carried out assuming drained conditions.
195
==PAGE:222==
Chapter 6. Numerical Model
6.1
Aksel Møllers Have
Aksel Møllers Have was chosen because the UCL is encountered at a relatively shallow depth
and displacement occurs within the layer. The wall has a length of 27m and an excavation
height of 23.7m with a width of 18.6m.
6.1.1
Design Inputs
The design inputs are based on that provided in the ‘Aksel Møllers Have-Permanent Structures
- Secant Pile Walls Calculation Report’.
6.1.1.1
Structural Elements
The model requires inputs for the structural elements interacting in the model such as the wall,
struts and top slab.
Retaining Wall
The required characteristics of the retaining wall are as follows:
• E: 33GPa
• Iw: 0.0599m4/m
• EI: 1.98x106kNm2/m
• L: 27m
Top Slab
The openings within the slab are taken into account and its characteristics are presented in the
calculation report as the design parameters:
• E: 35GPa
• Aeq: 0.67m2/m
• Ieq: 0.19m4/m
• EI: 6.70x106kNm2/m
• EA: 23.50106kN/m
• K: 1.30106kN/m/m
Struts
The characteristics of the struts are as follows:
• E: 210MPa
• Aeq: 0.011m2/m
• Ieq: 0.0048m4/m
• EI: 1.0x106kNm2/m
• EA: 2.40106kN/m
• K: 127103kN/m/m
There is also a 550kN prestressing force applied to each strut after installation, the equivalent
prestress force is deﬁned as:
Feq = 550kN/spacing = 550kN/5.5m = 110kN/m

6.1.1.2
Groundwater conditions
The ground water level was taken in accordance to the design as the Primary GWL at the SLS
in Table 4.3.5 (at z=+1.9m or -5.85m). As the excavation progresses, the GWT is lowered one
meter below the excavation - this has been accounted for in the modeling, as per Section 3.4.1.2.
6.1.1.3
Surface Loads
The load acting on the left side of the wall is taken as the backﬁlling between the average ground
level and the top of the pile (1.5m of backﬁll and γs = 20kN/m3 = 30kPa). The variable loads
were not included in the model including traﬃc loads and the loads resting on the top of the
slab during construction which are assumed to not have much inﬂuence in the response of the
structure.

The designer has chosen the operational elastic modulus for the retaining wall analysis as a
fraction of the elastic modulus at small strain. For soil layers, the stiﬀness ratio has been assessed
to be Evc/Emax = 1/5. Considering the soil proﬁle and the type of construction (installing the
roof slab prior to main excavation) and that the walls are deeply embedded in the limestone,
the structure is expected to be relatively stiﬀ, with soil strains at the middle to the lower limit
of the range indicated by Mair (1993), resulting in relatively high values of the ratio G/GD
(Metroselskabet, 2012g).

stages can be found in Appendix I.1.
Page 198
==PAGE:225==
Chapter 6. Numerical Model
Figure 6.1.2: Frew results and the measured lateral deformations at the end of Stage 10: Preparation
of the concrete level.
A wide overestimation of displacements at all layers is evident. At AMH-IN26, although the
displacements are widely larger than those recorded, the measurements at the UCL I layer dur-
ing Stages 4, 6 and 7 seem to match to those calculated in Frew, these stages correspond to the
installation of the struts, where a sharp increase of lateral pressure is introduced to the system.
The model fails to predict the slight rotation occurring after Stage 4 - at a depth of z≈-4m, above
the ﬁrst level of struts. Struts in Frew are modeled as springs and acts at a node located on the
wall axis and the connection between the wall and the strut may not represent the real behaviour.
The overestimation of displacements may be attributed to the soil stiﬀness (E) or the kr
parameter which relates the change in horizontal stress to a change in the vertical stress due to
unloading by means of the Poissons´s ratio as deﬁned in Section 3.4.1.3.
Analyses using the values of Emax and Eur in Table 6.1.1 were carried out in an eﬀort to
have a better understanding of the stiﬀness of the soil layers, the results with respect to the
measured displacements is shown in Figure 6.1.2.

Eur.
While the displacements within the limestone are still larger than those measured and show-

imation for the upper soil layers. The deﬂected shape still varies signiﬁcantly - the maximum
displacements for the model are recorded at a shallower depth of z=-10.0m when compared to
the ﬁeld observations.

be because the software does not capture the stiﬀness of the top slab correctly and does not
fully model the support this gives to the piles. In spite of the larger displacements predicted,
the forces acting in the struts are slightly smaller than those measured, as seen in Table 6.1.3,

6.1.3
Sensitivity Analysis
The model presented in Section 6.1.2 is a starting point of a series of parametric studies that
show the eﬀects of the soils parameters, mainly the stiﬀness, on the behaviour of the wall. The
sensitivity analysis is a study of how the variation in the output analyses of a model can be
attributed to variations of input parameters. As mentioned before, for the retaining structures
of the station the parameters that may aﬀect the behaviour signiﬁcantly are the following:
• E
′: Operational Young’s modulus;
• γ
′: Saturated unit weight;
• φ
′: Angle of shear resistance.
The designer carried out a sensitivity analysis and concluded that the variation of γ
′ and
φ
′ had negligible impact on the bending moments and lateral deﬂections of the wall (Metrosel-
skabet, 2012a). Based on these conclusions and the scope of this investigation, the parameters
investigated are the soil stiﬀness, E
′, and the parameter kr which is in turn dependent on the
Poisson’s ratio, υ.
The υ for the ML and UCL layer depends on the OCR which have been chosen as an upper
bound by the designer (Metroselskabet, 2012g) - a detailed description of the philosophy behind
the characteristic soil parameters is presented in Appendix D.2. Acknowledging the assessment
of the characteristic soil parameters, the lower bound of the υ for the UCL and MCL layers is then
considered. Furthermore, the displacements are largely overestimated in the ﬁrst three layers,
but are better described by the Eur and Emax. Based on the above, the following modiﬁcations
are considered:
• Modify the stiﬀness E for all layers to somewhere in between the E
′
vc and Emax;
• Adjust the kr parameter for the UCL layers.
Table 6.1.4 shows the geotechnical parameters used in the preliminary analysis. The modiﬁed
parameters are shown in red.

The stiﬀness of the ﬁrst three layers (FY, ML1 and DL) is increased. The stiﬀness for the
ML2 and UCL I remain unchanged while the Poisson’s ratio for the UCL I layer was decreased.
The elastic modulus for the UCL II and MCL are based on the Emass and Emax values pre-
sented in recent literature. Katic et al. (2014) found that low estimates of the elastic modulus
of the rock mass has been evaluated based on average induration as 800MPa. The authors also
found that the acoustic moduli from the ﬁeld and laboratory compared to the moduli obtained
by mechanical testing show the relation Emass/E0 ≈1/5 −1/3 is representative for the rock
mass stiﬀness.
Considering that the GSI of the UCL II is 55, it is safe to assume that the UCL II layer is
averagely indurated. The high stiﬀness of the MCL layer is based on the upper bound value for
Emax in Table D.1.3 and knowing that the displacements here are small (< 1mm), leading to
small strains. Lastly, the Poisson’s ratio for the limestone layers were reduced, to decrease the
curvature of the retaining wall occurring in these layers.

The lateral wall deformation for the end of Stage 10 using the parameters in Table 6.1.4 is shown
in Figure 6.1.4 and additional ﬁgures can be found in Appendix I.1.2.
There is slight overestimation of displacements in the DS and the UCL I during Stage 5.
While the modiﬁed parameters seem to agree with the measured displacements at AMH-IN27,
they fail to predict the larger displacements measured in AMH-IN26. Evidently, the behaviour
of the UCL II and MCL is stiﬀer than that considered by the designer.

capture the displacements closer to the UCL I at AMH-IN26. The following modiﬁcations are
made:
• Efit decrease for MCL to capture the wall’s curvature;
• kr increased at UCL I to inﬂuence the displacements due to unloading;

rameters, the soft response of the upper layers is not properly captured.

factors including the support connections with the retaining wall (top slab and struts) and their
stiﬀness may have a more signiﬁcant impact of that being captured by the numerical model.

(400MPa) is not supported by literature and practice, which list a minimum of 497MPa (see
Table D.1.2). The results for Stage 10 are shown in Figure 6.1.6, with respect to the previous
analysis. It is evident that the inward movement of the wall at he location of AMH-IN26 is
induced by other factors rather than the soil stiﬀness.

6.1.4
Conclusions
A sensitivity analysis was carried out for Aksel Møllers Have. The analyses conluded that the
limestone layer behaves stiﬀer than assumed by the designer who had chosen conservative values
of K0 which in turn aﬀect the Poisson ratio, based on the designer’s assessment.
Page 205
==PAGE:232==
Chapter 6. Numerical Model
The deep outward movement at the top of the wall may be attributed to relatively soft upper
soil layers and stiﬀsoils/rock at the lower part of the retaining wall. While the stiﬀness at the
upper most layers (FY, ML1, DS) seem to be higher than that proposed by the designer, the
evaluated stiﬀness (Fit #2) underestimates the displacements at the AMH-IN26 location.
The results using the parameters inf Table 6.1.5 (Figure 6.1.5) describe in good manner
AMH-25. However, this inclinometers was placed in a double secant pile wall and this might
have prevent the wall to deform naturally. AMH-IN27 is located close to the main excavation
corner and three-dimensional eﬀects may have inﬂuenced its behaviour.

Østerport

this layer are recognizable (δ > 2mm). The wall has a length of 27m and an excavation height

smaller predominantly in the middle strut, which carries the most load, however, strut levels
1 and 3 are comparable in magnitude. A possible explanation for the discrepancy in the strut
load is that the building in proximity to Østerport is not modeled and this added force would
have an impact on the retaining wall and consequently in the supporting temporary system.

6.2.3
Sensitivity Analysis
The model presented in Section 6.2.2 is a starting point of a series of parametric studies that
shows the eﬀects of the soils parameters on the behaviour of the wall. As mentioned before, for
the retaining structures of the station the parameters that may aﬀect the behaviour signiﬁcantly
are the following:
• E
′: Operational Young’s modulus;
• γ
′: Saturated unit weight;
• φ
′: Angle of shear resistance.
The designer carried out a sensitivity analysis and concluded that the variation of the γ
′
and φ
′ had negligible impact on the bending moments and lateral deﬂections of the wall (Met-
roselskabet, 2014a). Based on the designer conclusions and the scope of this investigation the
parameters investigated are the soil stiﬀness, E
′, and the parameter kr which is in turned de-
pendent on the Poisson’s ratio, υ.
The υ for the ML and UCL layer depend on the OCR which values have been considered
conservative by the designer (Metroselskabet, 2014c) - a detailed description of the philosophy
behind the characteristic soil parameters is presented in Appendix G.2.
Considering the assessment for the evaluation of the characteristic soil parameters, the lower
bound of the υ for the UCL and MCl layers is considered. Furthermore, it was observed that
the displacements are largely overestimated in the ﬁrst three layers, but are better described by
the Emax. Based on the above, the following modiﬁcations are considered:
• Modify the stiﬀness E to Emax
• Adjust the kr parameter for all layers
Table 6.2.4 shows the geotechnical parameters used in the sensitivity analysis. The modiﬁed
parameters are shown in red.
The elastic modulus for the UCL II and MCL are based on the Emass and Emax given by
recent studies. Katic et al. (2014) found that low estimates of the elastic modulus of the rock
mass has been evaluated based on average induration as 800MPa. The authors also found that
the relation Emass/E0 ≈1/5 −1/3 is representative for the rock mass stiﬀness.
Considering that the GSI of the UCL II is 55, is it safe to assume that the UCL II layer is
averagely indurated. The high stiﬀness of the MCL layer is based on the upper bound value for
Emax in Table G.1.3 and knowing that the displacements here are small (< 2mm), leading to
small strains.
